# [LIFT]{#id1.id1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps}: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks {#lift-language-interfaced-fine-tuning-for-non-language-machine-learning-tasks .ltx_title .ltx_title_document}

::: ltx_authors

:::

::: ltx_abstract
###### Abstract {#abstract .ltx_title .ltx_title_abstract}

Fine-tuning pretrained language models (LMs) without making any
architectural changes has become a norm for learning various language
downstream tasks. However, for *non*-language downstream tasks, a common
practice is to employ task-specific designs for input, output layers,
and loss functions. For instance, it is possible to fine-tune an LM into
an MNIST classifier by replacing the word embedding layer with an image
patch embedding layer, the word token output layer with a 10-way output
layer, and the word prediction loss with a 10-way classification loss,
respectively. A natural question arises: Can LM fine-tuning solve
non-language downstream tasks *without* changing the model architecture
or loss function? To answer this, we propose [Language-Interfaced
Fine-Tuning ([LIFT]{#id5.id1.3.1 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps})]{#id5.id1.3 .ltx_text .ltx_font_bold} and study
its efficacy and limitations by conducting an extensive empirical study
on a suite of non-language classification and regression tasks.
[LIFT]{#id5.id1.4 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} does not make
[any]{#id5.id1.5 .ltx_text .ltx_font_italic} changes to the model
architecture or loss function, and it solely relies on the natural
language interface, enabling "no-code machine learning with LMs." We
find that [LIFT]{#id5.id1.6 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} performs
comparably well across a wide range of low-dimensional classification
and regression tasks, matching the performances of the best baselines in
many cases, especially for the classification tasks. We also report
experimental results on the fundamental properties of [LIFT]{#id5.id1.7
.ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps}, including inductive bias, robustness,
and sample complexity. We also analyze the effect of pretraining on
[LIFT]{#id5.id1.8 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} and a few
properties/techniques specific to [LIFT]{#id5.id1.9 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps}, [e.g.,]{#id5.id1.10 .ltx_text .ltx_font_italic}
context-aware learning via appropriate prompting, calibrated
predictions, data generation, and two-stage fine-tuning. Our code is
available at
[https://github.com/UW-Madison-Lee-Lab/LanguageInterfacedFineTuning](https://github.com/UW-Madison-Lee-Lab/LanguageInterfacedFineTuning){.ltx_ref
.ltx_url .ltx_font_typewriter target="_blank"}. [{NoHyper}]{#id5.id1.11
.ltx_ERROR .undefined} [^†^[[^†^$^{*}$Equal contribution. Emails: Tuan
Dinh ([tuan.dinh@wisc.edu]{#footnote1.1 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_typewriter}), Yuchen Zeng ([yzeng58@wisc.edu]{#footnote1.2
.ltx_text
.ltx_font_typewriter})]{.ltx_note_content}]{.ltx_note_outer}]{#footnote1
.ltx_note .ltx_role_footnote}
:::

::: {#S1 .section .ltx_section}
## [1 ]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_section}Introduction {#introduction .ltx_title .ltx_title_section}

::: {#S1.p1 .ltx_para}
Deep neural networks have been highly successful across a multitude of
domains, from computer
vision \[[1](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib1){.ltx_ref},
[2](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib2){.ltx_ref}\]
and natural language
processing \[[3](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib3){.ltx_ref},
[4](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib4){.ltx_ref}\],
to game
playing \[[5](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib5){.ltx_ref},
[6](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib6){.ltx_ref}\].
Most advances in deep learning have come with a variety of
domain-specific designs for network architectures, such as convolutional
filters \[[7](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib7){.ltx_ref},
[8](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib8){.ltx_ref},
[9](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib9){.ltx_ref}\]
for vision tasks, or recurrent
modules \[[10](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib10){.ltx_ref},
[11](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib11){.ltx_ref}\]
and attention
mechanisms \[[12](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib12){.ltx_ref},
[13](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib13){.ltx_ref}\]
in the context of natural language processing. A domain-and-modality
agnostic model that can be adapted to solve tasks across different
modalities and domains has become a
desideratum \[[14](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib14){.ltx_ref}\],
motivating great efforts in transfer
learning \[[15](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib15){.ltx_ref}\]
and multi-modal
learning \[[16](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib16){.ltx_ref}\].
Recently, transformer-based language models
(LMs) \[[13](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib13){.ltx_ref},
[17](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib17){.ltx_ref},
[18](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib18){.ltx_ref},
[19](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib19){.ltx_ref}\]
exhibited impressive versatility across different domains and
modalities. They have shown great performances for various
language-based
tasks \[[20](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib20){.ltx_ref}\]
such as question
answering \[[21](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib21){.ltx_ref},
[22](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib22){.ltx_ref}\],
or commonsense
reasoning \[[23](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib23){.ltx_ref}\].
They have also been applied to non-language
modalities \[[18](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib18){.ltx_ref}\].
For instance,
GPT-2 \[[17](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib17){.ltx_ref}\]
pretrained on language data can be efficiently fine-tuned to perform
image classification and numerical
computation \[[18](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib18){.ltx_ref}\].
:::

<figure id="S1.F1" class="ltx_figure">
<img src="./2206.06565_files/prompts.png" id="S1.F1.g1"
class="ltx_graphics ltx_centering ltx_img_landscape" width="598"
height="233" alt="Refer to caption" />
<figcaption><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_figure"><span id="S1.F1.12.3.1"
class="ltx_text" style="font-size:90%;">Figure 1</span>: </span><span
id="S1.F1.4.2" class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" style="font-size:90%;">A
high-level illustration of the Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning (<span
id="S1.F1.4.2.3" class="ltx_text ltx_font_smallcaps">LIFT</span>)
framework.<span id="S1.F1.4.2.2" class="ltx_text ltx_font_medium"> <span
id="S1.F1.4.2.2.1" class="ltx_text ltx_font_smallcaps">LIFT</span> has a
two-phase procedure: (1) converting the dataset into sentences and (2)
fine-tuning the pretrained language model (<span id="S1.F1.4.2.2.2"
class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic">e.g.</span>, GPT) on the obtained
sentences. This figure visualizes how LIFT can be applied to the Iris
classification task. We first convert the Iris dataset into plain
English sentences (left). Since feature names and the task description
are available for this task, one could incorporate them as part of the
prompt (as option 1 in the figure). (In Sec. <a
href="https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#S4.SS1"
class="ltx_ref"
title="4.1 Does LIFT Benefit from Incorporating Feature Names? ‣ 4 Evaluation of LIFT-Specific Learning Properties ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"><span
class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">4.1</span></a>, we show that adding such
contextual information to prompts helps LIFT achieve higher predictive
accuracy.) One may also choose to use a simpler prompt with a generic
naming convention (<span
class="math inline"><em>x</em><sub>1</sub>, <em>x</em><sub>2</sub>, …, <em>x</em><sub><em>d</em></sub></span>)
for <span class="math inline"><em>p</em></span> features (as option 2 in
the figure). After the sentence conversion step, <span
id="S1.F1.4.2.2.3" class="ltx_text ltx_font_smallcaps">LIFT</span>
fine-tunes a pretrained LM with the sentence set without making any
changes to model architecture or loss. At inference time, we convert the
test samples to a sentence form using the same prompt, excluding the
label part. <span id="S1.F1.4.2.2.4"
class="ltx_text ltx_font_smallcaps">LIFT</span> performs surprisingly
well in various non-language regression/classification tasks, and we
summarize our main findings in Table <a
href="https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#S3.T1"
class="ltx_ref"
title="Table 1 ‣ 3.1 How Well Does LIFT Perform on Standard ML Tasks? ‣ 3 Basic Findings of LIFT ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"><span
class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">1</span></a>. Note that to obtain a model
for a given task, all we need here is to design proper sentence
templates for <span id="S1.F1.4.2.2.5"
class="ltx_text ltx_font_smallcaps">LIFT</span> and no changes to
architecture or loss functions are needed. </span></span></figcaption>
</figure>

::: {#S1.p2 .ltx_para}
When downstream tasks are language-based tasks, adapting pretrained LMs
can be achieved without modifying the models' architecture. Typically,
this adaptation is enabled via simple
fine-tuning \[[24](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib24){.ltx_ref},
[25](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib25){.ltx_ref},
[26](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib26){.ltx_ref},
[27](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib27){.ltx_ref}\]
or in-context few-shot learning
methods \[[28](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib28){.ltx_ref},
[29](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib29){.ltx_ref}\].
However, not altering the architecture may pose a limitation for
transferring to non-language tasks. As their input and output formats
are not in some language form, adapting LMs to these domains may seem to
require architectural changes. Indeed, it has been a common practice to
re-design the input/output layers and loss functions to accommodate a
different predictive task. For instance, to adapt
GPT-2 \[[21](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib21){.ltx_ref}\]
to other modalities, the frozen pretrained
transformer \[[18](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib18){.ltx_ref}\]
adds new input/output layers to handle different types of input/output.
To make such changes, one must have a good understanding of the
underlying principles of LMs and an ability to make proper modifications
at the code level.
:::

::: {#S1.p3 .ltx_para}
A natural question that arises is whether such changes are necessary. In
other words,\
[ [ [Does language model fine-tuning work for non-language
tasks]{#S1.p3.1.1.1.p1.1 .ltx_p} [[without]{#S1.p3.1.1.1.p1.2.1
.ltx_text .ltx_font_bold} changing the architecture or loss function at
all?]{#S1.p3.1.1.1.p1.2 .ltx_p} ]{#S1.p3.1.1.1.p1 .ltx_para
.ltx_align_center}]{#S1.p3.1.1.1 .ltx_inline-para .ltx_minipage
.ltx_align_middle .ltx_framed_rectangle style="width:426.8pt;"}
:::

::: {#S1.p4 .ltx_para}
To answer this, we consider a simple fine-tuning procedure for LMs,
referred to as [Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning ([LIFT]{#S1.p4.1.1.1
.ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps})]{#S1.p4.1.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_bold}.
This procedure can be used to learn predictors for any classification or
regression task. [LIFT]{#S1.p4.1.2 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} runs
in two phases: (1) converting labeled samples into sentences, and (2)
fine-tuning pretrained LMs on the sentence dataset without altering the
architecture or loss function.
:::

::: {#S1.p5 .ltx_para}
Fig. [[1]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#S1.F1 "Figure 1 ‣ 1 Introduction ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}
illustrates how we fine-tune GPT with [LIFT]{#S1.p5.2.2 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps} to solve the Iris classification
task \[[30](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib30){.ltx_ref}\].
[LIFT]{#S1.p5.2.3 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} first converts each
labeled sample into a sentence with two options. The first option is to
incorporate feature names and the task description into the sentence
template. In this example, we could convert a training sample
[r]{#S1.p5.2.4 .ltx_text .ltx_font_bold style="color:#BF0040;"} into "An
Iris plant with sepal length [r.sepal_length]{#S1.p5.2.5 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_bold style="color:#BF0040;"}, sepal width
[r.sepal_width]{#S1.p5.2.6 .ltx_text .ltx_font_bold
style="color:#BF0040;"}, petal length [r.petal_length]{#S1.p5.2.7
.ltx_text .ltx_font_bold style="color:#BF0040;"}, and petal width
[r.petal_width]{#S1.p5.2.8 .ltx_text .ltx_font_bold
style="color:#BF0040;"} is [r.class]{#S1.p5.2.9 .ltx_text .ltx_font_bold
style="color:#BF0040;"}." Here, we use the dot notation,
[i.e.,]{#S1.p5.2.10 .ltx_text .ltx_font_italic} [r.$\star$]{#S1.p5.1.1
.ltx_text .ltx_font_bold style="color:#BF0040;"} denotes the string
conversion of the corresponding attribute of sample [r]{#S1.p5.2.11
.ltx_text .ltx_font_bold style="color:#BF0040;"}. One may also adopt a
simpler (and more generic) sentence template, such as "If
x1=[r.x1]{#S1.p5.2.12 .ltx_text .ltx_font_bold style="color:#BF0040;"},
x2=[r.x2]{#S1.p5.2.13 .ltx_text .ltx_font_bold style="color:#BF0040;"},
..., xp=[r.xp]{#S1.p5.2.14 .ltx_text .ltx_font_bold
style="color:#BF0040;"}, then y=[r.y]{#S1.p5.2.15 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_bold style="color:#BF0040;"}," if there are $p$ features. We
then fine-tune LMs without changing either architecture or loss
function. Then, we perform inference as follows. [LIFT]{#S1.p5.2.16
.ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} first converts test samples into
sentences using the same template while leaving the prediction part
empty. It then feeds the converted sentences as prompts to the
fine-tuned model. The output tokens are parsed to provide the final
predictions.
:::

::: {#S1.p6 .ltx_para}
Our work empirically shows that [LIFT]{#S1.p6.1.1 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps} can provide high-accuracy solutions for a variety
of non-language tasks. Fig. [[2]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#S1.F2 "Figure 2 ‣ 1 Introduction ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}
shows examples of real functions learned by GPT-J
models \[[31](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib31){.ltx_ref}\]
fine-tuned using [LIFT]{#S1.p6.1.2 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} given
1000 samples. Recall that [LIFT]{#S1.p6.1.3 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps} does not require any changes in the architecture or
loss function, Thus, our findings show that such changes to
architecture/loss function might *not* be necessary, even when the
target predictive task is not a language task. Thus, [LIFT]{#S1.p6.1.5
.ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} can be almost perceived as a "no-code
machine learning" framework as the data-to-sentence conversion is
extremely straightforward even without extensive programming skills and
machine learning backgrounds.
:::

::: {#S1.p7 .ltx_para}
Motivated by these intriguing properties, we investigate the efficacy
and limitations of [LIFT]{#S1.p7.1.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} on
non-language tasks by conducting an extensive empirical study on a suite
of classification and regression tasks. [First]{#S1.p7.1.2 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_italic}, we observe that [LIFT]{#S1.p7.1.3 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps} performs well across a wide range of
low-dimensional classification and regression tasks. In most cases, it
nearly matches (or slightly outperforms) the best baselines'
performance. To further understand [LIFT]{#S1.p7.1.4 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps}, we conduct experiments testing the fundamental
learning properties, [e.g.,]{#S1.p7.1.5 .ltx_text .ltx_font_italic} its
inductive bias, sample efficiency, ability to extrapolate, worst- and
average-case noise robustness, and how the pretraining of LMs affects
[LIFT]{#S1.p7.1.6 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps}. [Third]{#S1.p7.1.7
.ltx_text .ltx_font_italic}, we study a few unique properties specific
to [LIFT]{#S1.p7.1.8 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps}, [e.g.,]{#S1.p7.1.9
.ltx_text .ltx_font_italic} context-aware learning with task-specific
prompting, prediction calibration, and the additional use of
[LIFT]{#S1.p7.1.10 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} for data generation.
[Lastly]{#S1.p7.1.11 .ltx_text .ltx_font_italic}, to improve upon the
basic fine-tuning, we employ a few techniques: two-stage fine-tuning
with synthetic pretext tasks and data augmentation. Both techniques
improve the performance of [LIFT]{#S1.p7.1.12 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps}. We finally provide discussions on limitations and
future investigations of [LIFT]{#S1.p7.1.13 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps}.
:::

::: {#S1.p8 .ltx_para}
[Scope of the study. ]{#S1.p8.1.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_bold} Our work
proposes the use of natural language interface for learning with LMs via
[LIFT]{#S1.p8.1.2 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps}. We emphasize that our
goal is *not* to achieve the state-of-the-art performance, but to
investigate thoroughly: (i) what [LIFT]{#S1.p8.1.4 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps} can and cannot do, (ii) properties of models
fine-tuned via [LIFT]{#S1.p8.1.5 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps}, and
(iii) whether we can improve [LIFT]{#S1.p8.1.6 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps} with advanced techniques.
:::

<figure id="S1.F2" class="ltx_figure">
<img src="./2206.06565_files/x1.png" id="S1.F2.g1"
class="ltx_graphics ltx_centering ltx_img_landscape" width="706"
height="235" alt="Refer to caption" />
<figcaption><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_figure"><span id="S1.F2.7.2.1"
class="ltx_text" style="font-size:90%;">Figure 2</span>: </span><span
id="S1.F2.2.1" class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold"
style="font-size:90%;">Approximating various functions with <span
id="S1.F2.2.1.2" class="ltx_text ltx_font_smallcaps">LIFT</span> using
GPT-J. <span id="S1.F2.2.1.1" class="ltx_text ltx_font_medium"> We
visualize the target functions (first row) and the predictor functions
learned by <span id="S1.F2.2.1.1.1"
class="ltx_text ltx_font_smallcaps">LIFT</span> on GPT-J (second row).
Blue dots show the <span class="math inline">1000</span> training
samples. One can observe that <span id="S1.F2.2.1.1.2"
class="ltx_text ltx_font_smallcaps">LIFT</span> well approximates the
target functions. </span></span></figcaption>
</figure>
:::

::: {#S2 .section .ltx_section}
## [2 ]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_section}Methodology and Experimental Setup {#methodology-and-experimental-setup .ltx_title .ltx_title_section}

::: {#S2.p1 .ltx_para}
[LIFT[ training. ]{#S2.p1.2.1.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_upright}]{#S2.p1.2.1
.ltx_text .ltx_font_bold .ltx_font_smallcaps} To fine-tune a pretrained
LM with [LIFT]{#S2.p1.2.2 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} on a target
supervised learning task, we apply two steps: (1) convert each sample
into a sentence with a fixed template, and (2) fine-tune LMs with
sentence datasets. We use the default cross-entropy loss for token
prediction in LMs. Our generic template (without feature names and task
description) for sample [r]{#S2.p1.2.3 .ltx_text .ltx_font_bold
style="color:#BF0040;"} is

  -- ----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- --
     $${\underset{\text{question}}{\underbrace{\text{When we have x1=}\textbf{r.x1}\text{, x2=}\textbf{r.x2}\text{, …, xp=}\textbf{r.xp}\text{, what should be y?}}}\operatorname{}\underset{\text{q/a separator}}{\underbrace{\#\#\#}}\operatorname{}\underset{\text{answer}}{\underbrace{y=\textbf{r.y}}}\operatorname{}\underset{\text{end of answer}}{\underbrace{\text{@@@}}}},$$   
  -- ----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- --

if [r]{#S2.p1.1.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_bold style="color:#BF0040;"} has
$p$ attributes. Here, we use the separator convention recommended by
OpenAI \[[32](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib32){.ltx_ref}\]
-- "###" for question/answer separation, and "[@@@]{#S2.p1.1.2 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_typewriter}" for end of generation. When attributes names and
task descriptions are available, one can use a more informative prompt
(shown in Fig. [[1]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#S1.F1 "Figure 1 ‣ 1 Introduction ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref})
with all actual prompts are provided in Sec. [[4.1]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#S4.SS1 "4.1 Does LIFT Benefit from Incorporating Feature Names? ‣ 4 Evaluation of LIFT-Specific Learning Properties ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}.
We report learning curves of [LIFT]{#S2.p1.1.3 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps} on several tasks in Appendix [[E.5]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A5.SS5 "E.5 LIFT’s Training Curve ‣ Appendix E Additional Experiments and Findings (NOT Discussed in the Main Paper) ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}.
:::

::: {#S2.p2 .ltx_para}
[LIFT[ inference. ]{#S2.p2.2.1.1 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_upright}]{#S2.p2.2.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_bold
.ltx_font_smallcaps} For inference, we use the same prompt template
except for the answer and end-of-answer parts. Once the fine-tuned LM
completes the test prompt, we simply parse the output tokens. For
classification, we simply compare the generated text with the string
representation of the class names. For regression, we convert the
generated string into a number. For instance, with the output being
"$y$=10.35[@@@extratokens]{#S2.p2.2.2 .ltx_text .ltx_font_typewriter}",
we split the output sentence by the stop separator "[@@@]{#S2.p2.2.3
.ltx_text .ltx_font_typewriter}" into two parts. Taking the first part
"$y$=10.35", we parse the value "10.35" as our final prediction.
:::

::: {#S2.p3 .ltx_para}
The generated output might be [invalid]{#S2.p3.2.1 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_italic}. For classification tasks, the output string may not
match any actual class, which we declare as misclassification. Note that
one may obtain better accuracy by returning its closest class using
string metrics. For regression tasks, we consider output invalid if the
string-to-number parsing fails. In these cases, we adjust the generation
randomness by increasing the decoding
temperature \[[33](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib33){.ltx_ref},
[34](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib34){.ltx_ref},
[35](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib35){.ltx_ref}\]
from $0$ (deterministic mode) to $0.75$ (random mode). We repeat the
inference up to five times, then return the average value of the
training set if all attempts fail. Note that invalid output occurs very
rarely (less than or around 1 in most tested cases).
:::

::: {#S2.p4 .ltx_para}
For evaluation metrics, we use accuracy for classification tasks, and
RMSE, RAE errors for regression tasks, where
$\text{RAE} = {\sum_{i = 1}^{n}{{|{{\hat{y}}_{i} - y_{i}}|}/{\sum_{i = 1}^{n}{|{{\frac{1}{n}\operatorname{}{\sum_{j = 1}^{n}y_{j}}} - y_{i}}|}}}}$
and
$\text{RMSE} = \sqrt{\sum_{i = 1}^{n}{{({{\hat{y}}_{i} - y_{i}})}^{2}/n}}$
on each dataset
$\mathcal{D} = {\{{({\mathbf{x}}_{i},y_{i})}\}}_{i = 1}^{n}$ with $n$
samples, features
${\mathbf{x}} \in \mathcal{X} \subset {\mathbb{R}}^{p}$, and outcome
$y$.
:::

::: {#S2.p5 .ltx_para}
[Pretrained LMs. ]{#S2.p5.1.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_bold} We apply
[LIFT]{#S2.p5.1.2 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} on two pretrained LMs:
GPT-J \[[31](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib31){.ltx_ref}\]
and
GPT-3 \[[19](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib19){.ltx_ref}\].
To fine-tune GPT-J, we use
LoRA \[[24](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib24){.ltx_ref}\],
a parameter-efficient method that constrains weight matrix updates to be
low-rank. For experiments on GPT-J, we used [p3.8xlarge]{#S2.p5.1.3
.ltx_text .ltx_font_typewriter} and [p3.2xlarge]{#S2.p5.1.4 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_typewriter} instances from AWS and RTX3090 GPUs in the local
server. Since GPT-3 is not fully publicly available, we use the API
provided by OpenAI to perform black-box GPT-3 fine-tuning. More details
are in Appendix [[C.2.1]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A3.SS2.SSS1 "C.2.1 Pretrained Language Models and Baselines ‣ C.2 LIFT and Baseline Implementation ‣ Appendix C Experimental Setup ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}.
:::

::: {#S2.p6 .ltx_para}
[Datasets. ]{#S2.p6.10.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_bold} We design and select
a wide range of datasets to better understand the behavior of
[LIFT]{#S2.p6.10.2 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps}. For
[classification]{#S2.p6.10.3 .ltx_text .ltx_font_italic}, we use three
types of non-language data: low-dimensional synthetic datasets, real
tabular datasets in
OpenML \[[36](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib36){.ltx_ref}\],
and vision datasets
(MNIST \[[37](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib37){.ltx_ref}\],
Fashion-MNIST \[[38](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib38){.ltx_ref}\]
and their permuted
variants \[[39](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib39){.ltx_ref}\]).
For [regression]{#S2.p6.10.4 .ltx_text .ltx_font_italic}, we use both
synthetic and real datasets. For synthetic datasets, we defined samples
$({\mathbf{x}}_{i},y_{i})$ of input-output pair as
$\text{y} \sim {{f\operatorname{}{(\text{x})}} + {\mathcal{N}\operatorname{}{(0,\sigma^{2})}}}$,
where $\sigma^{2} \geq 0$ is the noise level. Unless otherwise stated,
we sample the feature [x]{#S2.p6.10.5 .ltx_text .ltx_markedasmath}
uniformly from a hypercube ${\lbrack L,U\rbrack}^{p}$, where $L$ and $U$
are minimum/maximum feature values, and $p$ is the number of features.
Following the suggestion
by \[[40](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib40){.ltx_ref}\],
we consider various functions $f$ for regression tasks: (i) linear
function, (ii) quadratic function, (iii) exponential function, (iv)
cosine function, (v) $\ell$`<!-- -->`{=html}1-norm function, and (vi)
piece-wise linear function. Their 2D visualizations are provided in the
first row of Fig. [[2]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#S1.F2 "Figure 2 ‣ 1 Introduction ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}.
We also use four real datasets: Medical Insurance
(Insurance) \[[41](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib41){.ltx_ref}\],
Combined Cycle Power Plant
(CCPP) \[[42](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib42){.ltx_ref}\],
Servo \[[43](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib43){.ltx_ref}\],
and Student Performance
(Student) \[[44](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib44){.ltx_ref}\].
More details are included in Appendix [[C.1]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A3.SS1 "C.1 Datasets ‣ Appendix C Experimental Setup ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}.
:::

::: {#S2.p7 .ltx_para}
[Baselines. ]{#S2.p7.1.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_bold} We consider standard
learning
algorithms \[[45](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib45){.ltx_ref},
[46](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib46){.ltx_ref}\].
For classification, we use logistic regression ([LogReg]{#S2.p7.1.2
.ltx_text .ltx_font_italic}), decision tree ([DT]{#S2.p7.1.3 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_italic}), k-nearest neighbor ([KNN]{#S2.p7.1.4 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_italic}), support vector machine with Gaussian kernel
([SVM]{#S2.p7.1.5 .ltx_text .ltx_font_italic}), a four-layer ReLU neural
network ([MLP]{#S2.p7.1.6 .ltx_text .ltx_font_italic}) with 200 neurons
per hidden layer, random forest ([RF]{#S2.p7.1.7 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_italic}), and XGBoost ([XG]{#S2.p7.1.8 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_italic}). We also use the majority class classifier
([MCC]{#S2.p7.1.9 .ltx_text .ltx_font_italic}) that outputs the most
dominant class. For regression, we use polynomial regression
([PR]{#S2.p7.1.10 .ltx_text .ltx_font_italic}), kernel ridge regression
([KR]{#S2.p7.1.11 .ltx_text .ltx_font_italic}) with radial basis
function kernel, $k$-nearest neighbors ([KNN]{#S2.p7.1.12 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_italic}), a three-layer ReLU neural network ([MLP]{#S2.p7.1.13
.ltx_text .ltx_font_italic}) with 50 hidden neurons per each layer,
Gradient Boosting Trees ([GBT]{#S2.p7.1.14 .ltx_text .ltx_font_italic}),
random forest ([RF]{#S2.p7.1.15 .ltx_text .ltx_font_italic}), and
Gaussian process ([GP]{#S2.p7.1.16 .ltx_text .ltx_font_italic}). For
hyperparameter selection, we apply the grid search on a set of
parameters' values and use cross-validation on the training set (see
details in Appendix [[C.2]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A3.SS2 "C.2 LIFT and Baseline Implementation ‣ Appendix C Experimental Setup ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}).
:::
:::

::: {#S3 .section .ltx_section}
## [3 ]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_section}Basic Findings of [LIFT]{#S3.1.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} {#basic-findings-of-lift .ltx_title .ltx_title_section}

::: {#S3.p1 .ltx_para}
Table [[1]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#S3.T1 "Table 1 ‣ 3.1 How Well Does LIFT Perform on Standard ML Tasks? ‣ 3 Basic Findings of LIFT ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}
summarizes our main findings. We also study sample complexity
(Sec. [[3.2]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#S3.SS2 "3.2 How Many Samples Does LIFT Need? ‣ 3 Basic Findings of LIFT ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}),
comparison with in-context learning (Sec. [[3.3]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#S3.SS3 "3.3 Language-Interfaced Learning: LIFT versus In-Context Learning (ICL) ‣ 3 Basic Findings of LIFT ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}),
models' decision boundaries (Sec. [[3.4]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#S3.SS4 "3.4 Can We Understand the Inductive Biases of Language Models via LIFT? ‣ 3 Basic Findings of LIFT ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}),
and the effect of LMs' pretraining on [LIFT]{#S3.p1.1.1 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps} (Sec. [[3.6]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#S3.SS6 "3.6 Does LIFT Need Large-Scale Models Pretrained on Natural Language Data? ‣ 3 Basic Findings of LIFT ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}).
Appendix [[E]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A5 "Appendix E Additional Experiments and Findings (NOT Discussed in the Main Paper) ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}
provides additional results, including the effect of input and output
layers ([[E.1]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A5.SS1 "E.1 What Is the Effect of Replacing the Input or Output Layers? ‣ Appendix E Additional Experiments and Findings (NOT Discussed in the Main Paper) ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}),
model size ([[E.2]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A5.SS2 "E.2 Does LIFT Benefit from Larger LMs? ‣ Appendix E Additional Experiments and Findings (NOT Discussed in the Main Paper) ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}),
and [LIFT]{#S3.p1.1.2 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} for Ridge
regression ([[E.4]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A5.SS4 "E.4 Can LIFT Perform Ridge Regression via Data Augmentation? ‣ Appendix E Additional Experiments and Findings (NOT Discussed in the Main Paper) ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}).
:::

::: {#S3.SS1 .section .ltx_subsection}
### [3.1 ]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_subsection}How Well Does [LIFT]{#S3.SS1.1.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} Perform on Standard ML Tasks? {#how-well-does-lift-perform-on-standard-ml-tasks .ltx_title .ltx_title_subsection}

::: {#S3.SS1.p1 .ltx_para}
[Classification. ]{#S3.SS1.p1.2.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_bold}
Table [[2]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#S3.T2 "Table 2 ‣ 3.1 How Well Does LIFT Perform on Standard ML Tasks? ‣ 3 Basic Findings of LIFT ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}
compares classification accuracies between algorithms on a wide range of
tasks. We observe that [LIFT]{#S3.SS1.p1.2.2 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps} achieves comparable performance to most baselines.
In most cases, [LIFT]{#S3.SS1.p1.2.3 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps}/GPT
ranks highly in the top three best-performing methods. We find that
[LIFT]{#S3.SS1.p1.2.4 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} can learn
non-linear relationships between features and the responses:
[LIFT]{#S3.SS1.p1.2.5 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps}/GPT-3 achieves
accuracy on the circle dataset, while logistic regression
failed to perform better than the MCC ($50\%)$. As the difficulty of
tasks varies, which can be estimated by the average performance of
baselines, [LIFT]{#S3.SS1.p1.2.6 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} also
suffers from performance degradation. [LIFT]{#S3.SS1.p1.2.7 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps} can perform comparably well even when the number of
features is as large as hundreds, though the limited number of tokens as
inputs to LMs restricts the number of features [LIFT]{#S3.SS1.p1.2.8
.ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} can input. However, when the number of
classes is large (say 100s), both [LIFT]{#S3.SS1.p1.2.9 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps}/GPT models have lower accuracies than many
baselines, though they manage to be better than MCC. For instance, on
the 100-class Margin dataset, the accuracy gap between
[LIFT]{#S3.SS1.p1.2.10 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps}/GPT-J and the best
algorithm (RBF-SVM) is nearly . Note that [LIFT]{#S3.SS1.p1.2.11
.ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} can directly use raw data while most
baselines require feature scaling and normalization for good
performance. More results are provided in Appendix [[D.1.1]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A4.SS1.SSS1 "D.1.1 How Well Does LIFT Perform on Standard ML Tasks? ‣ D.1 Results for Basic Findings of LIFT (Section 3) ‣ Appendix D Detailed and Extended Results of Experiments in the Main Paper ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref},
including comparisons with methods leveraging larger models.
:::

<figure id="S3.T1" class="ltx_table">
<figcaption><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_table"><span id="S3.T1.3.1.1"
class="ltx_text" style="font-size:90%;">Table 1</span>: </span><span
id="S3.T1.4.2" class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold"
style="font-size:90%;">Summary of the main findings.<span
id="S3.T1.4.2.1" class="ltx_text ltx_font_medium">
</span></span></figcaption>
</figure>


class="ltx_text ltx_font_smallcaps">LIFT</span>/GPTs on 2D synthetic
data, tabular data in OpenML [<a
href="https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib36"
class="ltx_ref">36</a>], and image data, varying number of features
(<span class="math inline"><em>p</em></span>) and data classes (<span
class="math inline"><em>c</em></span>). Overall, <span id="S3.T2.8.4.3"
class="ltx_text ltx_font_smallcaps">LIFT</span>/GPTs perform comparably
well across tasks, adapting to non-linear datasets (circles, two
circles) beyond the capacity of logistic regression. For OpenML
datasets, they achieve competitive performances with the best methods,
<span id="S3.T2.8.4.4" class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic">e.g.</span>,
XGBoost). The performance degrades when more classes are given, <span
id="S3.T2.8.4.5" class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic">e.g.</span>, <span
class="math inline"><em>c</em></span>=100. They achieve competitive
accuracies on both MNIST and Fashion MNIST. Note that MNIST’s classes
are not fully balanced; thus, MCC achieves 11.35% instead of 10%.
Table <a href="https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A4.T10"
class="ltx_ref"
title="Table 10 ‣ D.1.1 How Well Does LIFT Perform on Standard ML Tasks? ‣ D.1 Results for Basic Findings of LIFT (Section 3) ‣ Appendix D Detailed and Extended Results of Experiments in the Main Paper ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"><span
class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">10</span></a> provides the full comparison
with all baselines (KNN, MLP, Random Forest). </span></figcaption>
</figure>

::: {#S3.SS1.p2 .ltx_para}
[Regression. ]{#S3.SS1.p2.1.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_bold}
Tables [[12]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A4.T12 "Table 12 ‣ Regression. ‣ D.1.1 How Well Does LIFT Perform on Standard ML Tasks? ‣ D.1 Results for Basic Findings of LIFT (Section 3) ‣ Appendix D Detailed and Extended Results of Experiments in the Main Paper ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}
and [[13]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A4.T13 "Table 13 ‣ Regression. ‣ D.1.1 How Well Does LIFT Perform on Standard ML Tasks? ‣ D.1 Results for Basic Findings of LIFT (Section 3) ‣ Appendix D Detailed and Extended Results of Experiments in the Main Paper ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}
present our [function approximation]{#S3.SS1.p2.1.2 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_italic} comparison. For the low-dimensional cases,
[LIFT]{#S3.SS1.p2.1.3 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} is comparable to
baselines. Still, it fails to beat the strongest baselines, such as GP,
as GPT models measure the error by comparing tokens instead of measuring
how close the prediction values are to true values. We conjecture that
we can improve our performance by level encoding, [i.e.]{#S3.SS1.p2.1.4
.ltx_text .ltx_font_italic}, representing numerical values as binary
values. We also investigate the [interpolation and
extrapolation]{#S3.SS1.p2.1.5 .ltx_text .ltx_font_italic} of
[LIFT]{#S3.SS1.p2.1.6 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} and defer the
details to Sec. [[D.1.1]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A4.SS1.SSS1 "D.1.1 How Well Does LIFT Perform on Standard ML Tasks? ‣ D.1 Results for Basic Findings of LIFT (Section 3) ‣ Appendix D Detailed and Extended Results of Experiments in the Main Paper ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}.
All methods fail to extrapolate and interpolate well for all functions,
and the interpolation performance of [LIFT]{#S3.SS1.p2.1.7 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps} is only good in the linear regression case.
Interestingly, [LIFT]{#S3.SS1.p2.1.8 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps}
tends to output seen values (from training data) for extrapolation.
:::
:::

::: {#S3.SS2 .section .ltx_subsection}
### [3.2 ]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_subsection}How Many Samples Does [LIFT]{#S3.SS2.1.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} Need? {#how-many-samples-does-lift-need .ltx_title .ltx_title_subsection}

::: {#S3.SS2.p1 .ltx_para}
We investigate whether [LIFT]{#S3.SS2.p1.1.1 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps} is sample efficient. Fig. [[12]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A4.F12 "Figure 12 ‣ D.1.2 How Many Samples Does LIFT Need? ‣ D.1 Results for Basic Findings of LIFT (Section 3) ‣ Appendix D Detailed and Extended Results of Experiments in the Main Paper ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}
in Appendix shows the sample complexity evaluation on classification and
regression tasks. We find that GPT models can be quickly fine-tuned to
learn new tasks with [LIFT]{#S3.SS2.p1.1.2 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps}. For [classification]{#S3.SS2.p1.1.3 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_italic}, as the number of classes increases (left to right
columns in Fig. [[12]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A4.F12 "Figure 12 ‣ D.1.2 How Many Samples Does LIFT Need? ‣ D.1 Results for Basic Findings of LIFT (Section 3) ‣ Appendix D Detailed and Extended Results of Experiments in the Main Paper ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}a),
[LIFT]{#S3.SS2.p1.1.4 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} does need more
samples for adaptation, probably because the data input and output
spaces are more complex to learn. For [regression]{#S3.SS2.p1.1.5
.ltx_text .ltx_font_italic} tasks, we find that $1000$ samples are
sufficient for [LIFT]{#S3.SS2.p1.1.6 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} to
have a small RMSE, similar to other baselines. There exist some
functions ([e.g.]{#S3.SS2.p1.1.7 .ltx_text .ltx_font_italic}, cosine and
piecewise) where [LIFT]{#S3.SS2.p1.1.8 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps}
has lower sample complexity than popular baselines.
:::
:::

::: {#S3.SS3 .section .ltx_subsection}
### [3.3 ]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_subsection}Language-Interfaced Learning: [LIFT]{#S3.SS3.1.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} versus In-Context Learning (ICL) {#language-interfaced-learning-lift-versus-in-context-learning-icl .ltx_title .ltx_title_subsection}

<figure id="S3.T3" class="ltx_table">
<figcaption><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_table"><span id="S3.T3.45.2.1"
class="ltx_text" style="font-size:90%;">Table 3</span>: </span><span
id="S3.T3.2.1" class="ltx_text" style="font-size:90%;"> <span
id="S3.T3.2.1.1" class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold">Comparison of accuracies
(<span class="math inline">↑</span>) between ICL and fine-tuning with
<span id="S3.T3.2.1.1.1" class="ltx_text ltx_font_smallcaps">LIFT</span>
on OpenML datasets.</span> “<span id="S3.T3.2.1.2"
class="ltx_text ltx_font_smallcaps">LIFT</span>/Full-Data” and “<span
id="S3.T3.2.1.3" class="ltx_text ltx_font_smallcaps">LIFT</span>/Subset”
represent <span id="S3.T3.2.1.4"
class="ltx_text ltx_font_smallcaps">LIFT</span> on the full dataset and
and its subset used correspondingly in the ICL setting (number of
prompts). Here, the size of subset is chosen to satisfy the LMs’ context
length. Overall, <span id="S3.T3.2.1.5"
class="ltx_text ltx_font_smallcaps">LIFT</span>/GPTs on full data
achieve the best performances. However, when using the same number of
samples, <span id="S3.T3.2.1.6"
class="ltx_text ltx_font_smallcaps">LIFT</span> and ICL are more
comparable in most cases. Note that both methods may be worse than MCC
due to the limited training data in some cases. </span></figcaption>
</figure>

::: {#S3.SS3.p1 .ltx_para}
Beyond fine-tuning (with [LIFT]{#S3.SS3.p1.2.1 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps}), our language-interfaced learning framework can be
used for other learning methods for LMs, including in-context learning
(ICL) \[[47](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib47){.ltx_ref},
[48](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib48){.ltx_ref},
[19](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib19){.ltx_ref}\]
that performs inference on new tasks without fine-tuning by conditioning
on a few training examples. Table [[3]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#S3.T3 "Table 3 ‣ 3.3 Language-Interfaced Learning: LIFT versus In-Context Learning (ICL) ‣ 3 Basic Findings of LIFT ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}
compares the classification performances between (a) ICL, (b)
[LIFT]{#S3.SS3.p1.2.2 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} trained on a subset
with $n$ samples, and (c) [LIFT]{#S3.SS3.p1.2.3 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps} trained on the full dataset. Note that the number
of training samples ($n$) used for ICL depends on the context length of
given LMs. As we can see, [LIFT]{#S3.SS3.p1.2.4 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps} using the full dataset always achieves the best
performances. However, [LIFT]{#S3.SS3.p1.2.5 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps}/Subset and ICL are more comparable in most cases
when they use the same number of training samples, which are
sufficiently small for ICL methods to fit in LMs.
:::

::: {#Thmremarkx1 .ltx_theorem .ltx_theorem_remark}
###### [[Remark]{#Thmremarkx1.1.1.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_bold}]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_theorem}[.]{#Thmremarkx1.2.2 .ltx_text .ltx_font_bold} {#remark. .ltx_title .ltx_runin .ltx_title_theorem}

::: {#Thmremarkx1.p1 .ltx_para}
[One can replace fine-tuning with ICL in our language-interfaced
procedure when the target tasks require fewer training
samples.]{#Thmremarkx1.p1.1.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_italic}
:::
:::

<figure id="S3.F3" class="ltx_figure ltx_align_floatright">
<img src="./2206.06565_files/decision_boundary_clf_v3.png" id="S3.F3.g1"
class="ltx_graphics ltx_centering ltx_img_landscape" width="281"
height="179" alt="Refer to caption" />
<figcaption><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_figure"><span id="S3.F3.4.1.1"
class="ltx_text" style="font-size:90%;">Figure 3</span>: </span><span
id="S3.F3.5.2" class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold"
style="font-size:90%;">Decision boundary visualization.<span
id="S3.F3.5.2.1" class="ltx_text ltx_font_medium"> We use three
snapshots of a trained network to construct datasets having labels as
their predictions (the first column). Top to bottom: snapshots with more
training epochs, corresponding to more complex boundaries. <span
id="S3.F3.5.2.1.1" class="ltx_text ltx_font_smallcaps">LIFT</span>/GPTs
adapt well on different boundaries. </span></span></figcaption>
</figure>
:::

::: {#S3.SS4 .section .ltx_subsection}
### [3.4 ]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_subsection}Can We Understand the Inductive Biases of Language Models via [LIFT]{#S3.SS4.1.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps}? {#can-we-understand-the-inductive-biases-of-language-models-via-lift .ltx_title .ltx_title_subsection}

::: {#S3.SS4.p1 .ltx_para}
To better understand [LIFT]{#S3.SS4.p1.1.1 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps}/GPTs' inductive biases, we investigate their
classification decision boundaries varying the boundaries' complexity,
as shown in Fig. [[3]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#S3.F3 "Figure 3 ‣ 3.3 Language-Interfaced Learning: LIFT versus In-Context Learning (ICL) ‣ 3 Basic Findings of LIFT ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}.
We first train a binary-class neural network and use its snapshots at
different training epochs to construct datasets having decision
boundaries at different complexity levels (first column in
Fig. [[3]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#S3.F3 "Figure 3 ‣ 3.3 Language-Interfaced Learning: LIFT versus In-Context Learning (ICL) ‣ 3 Basic Findings of LIFT ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}).
We observe that [LIFT]{#S3.SS4.p1.1.2 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps}/GPT
models adapt well to three boundaries and capture their rough shapes.
Furthermore, their boundary shapes are axis-parallel, similar to the
boundary of tree-based classifiers. They also show a lot of fractals
similar to the observations on some convolution neural
networks \[[49](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib49){.ltx_ref}\].
See Appendix [[D.1.3]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A4.SS1.SSS3 "D.1.3 Can We Understand the Inductive Biases of Language Models via LIFT? ‣ D.1 Results for Basic Findings of LIFT (Section 3) ‣ Appendix D Detailed and Extended Results of Experiments in the Main Paper ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}
for results of 3-class and 5-class datasets and quantitative
measurements.
:::
:::

::: {#S3.SS5 .section .ltx_subsection}
### [3.5 ]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_subsection}How Robust is [LIFT]{#S3.SS5.1.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps}? {#how-robust-is-lift .ltx_title .ltx_title_subsection}

::: {#S3.SS5.p1 .ltx_para}
We investigate the robustness of [LIFT]{#S3.SS5.p1.1.1 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps} against the outlier samples in training data and
the feature corruption on test data. Appendix [[D.1.4]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A4.SS1.SSS4 "D.1.4 How Robust Is LIFT? ‣ D.1 Results for Basic Findings of LIFT (Section 3) ‣ Appendix D Detailed and Extended Results of Experiments in the Main Paper ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}
provides additional experimental results, including the robustness for
the case of label corruption on training data and class-imbalanced data.
:::

::: {#S3.SS5.p2 .ltx_para}
[Robustness to outliers in training data. ]{#S3.SS5.p2.2.1 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_bold} We consider regression tasks where we have outliers
whose outcome $y$ is not consistent with the majority of samples in
terms of fitting $({\mathbf{x}},y)$. Fig. [[14]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A4.F14 "Figure 14 ‣ Robustness to outliers in training data. ‣ D.1.4 How Robust Is LIFT? ‣ D.1 Results for Basic Findings of LIFT (Section 3) ‣ Appendix D Detailed and Extended Results of Experiments in the Main Paper ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}a
compares RAE values of methods with and without outliers (2% outliers in
the training set). [LIFT]{#S3.SS5.p2.2.2 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps}/GPT models are among the most robust ones: their
performances are almost unaffected, while baselines suffer huge
performance drops. Furthermore, we evaluate models under various
percentages of outliers (1%, 2%, 5%, 10%, 20%), as shown in
Fig. [[14]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A4.F14 "Figure 14 ‣ Robustness to outliers in training data. ‣ D.1.4 How Robust Is LIFT? ‣ D.1 Results for Basic Findings of LIFT (Section 3) ‣ Appendix D Detailed and Extended Results of Experiments in the Main Paper ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}b.
Compared to the robust baselines (median-3NN and
median-5NN) \[[50](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib50){.ltx_ref}\],
[LIFT]{#S3.SS5.p2.2.3 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps}/GPT-3 is comparably
robust, while [LIFT]{#S3.SS5.p2.2.4 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps}/GPT-J
is more vulnerable when more outliers are present.
:::

<figure id="S3.T4" class="ltx_table">
<div id="S3.T4.6"
class="ltx_inline-block ltx_align_center ltx_transformed_outer"
style="width:151.8pt;height:148.9pt;vertical-align:-0.0pt;">
<span class="ltx_transformed_inner"
style="transform:translate(20.9pt,-20.5pt) scale(1.37893647884252,1.37893647884252) ;">
</span>
</div>
<figcaption><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_table"><span id="S3.T4.13.2.1"
class="ltx_text" style="font-size:129%;">Table 4</span>: </span><span
id="S3.T4.2.1" class="ltx_text" style="font-size:129%;"> <span
id="S3.T4.2.1.1" class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold">Accuracies (<span
class="math inline">↑</span>) under the perturbation on the input
feature of MNIST data.</span> See the full results in Table <a
href="https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A4.T18"
class="ltx_ref"
title="Table 18 ‣ Robustness to feature corruption on test data. ‣ D.1.4 How Robust Is LIFT? ‣ D.1 Results for Basic Findings of LIFT (Section 3) ‣ Appendix D Detailed and Extended Results of Experiments in the Main Paper ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"><span
class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">18</span></a> in Appendix <a
href="https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A4.SS1.SSS4"
class="ltx_ref"
title="D.1.4 How Robust Is LIFT? ‣ D.1 Results for Basic Findings of LIFT (Section 3) ‣ Appendix D Detailed and Extended Results of Experiments in the Main Paper ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"><span
class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">D.1.4</span></a>. </span></figcaption>
</figure>

::: {#S3.SS5.p3 .ltx_para}
[Robustness to feature corruption on test data. ]{#S3.SS5.p3.7.1
.ltx_text .ltx_font_bold} Given a clean test data $({\mathbf{x}},y)$
having feature $\mathbf{x}$ and label $y$, we explore whether adding
small perturbation $\mathbf{δ}$ on the feature changes the performance;
we measure the accuracy of [LIFT]{#S3.SS5.p3.7.2 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps} on perturbed data
$({{\mathbf{x}} + {\mathbf{δ}}},y)$. We apply transfer
attack \[[51](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib51){.ltx_ref}\]
since we do not have full access to the GPT-3 model, and finding
adversarial examples in the discrete input space is
complex \[[52](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib52){.ltx_ref}\].
Table [[4]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#S3.T4 "Table 4 ‣ 3.5 How Robust is LIFT? ‣ 3 Basic Findings of LIFT ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}
reports robustness results on MNIST classification under PGD attacks
transferred from LeNet-5. The perturbation radius is set to
$\varepsilon \in {\lbrack 0,0.01,0.1,0.3\rbrack}$ where MNIST pixel
value is within \[0,1\]. We compare three networks: LeNet-5, MLP (2
hidden layers with 300 and 100 neurons), and [LIFT]{#S3.SS5.p3.7.3
.ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps}/GPT-3. When
$\epsilon \in {\{ 0.01,0.1\}}$, [LIFT]{#S3.SS5.p3.7.4 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps}/GPT-3 tolerates random noise (as in
Table [[18]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A4.T18 "Table 18 ‣ Robustness to feature corruption on test data. ‣ D.1.4 How Robust Is LIFT? ‣ D.1 Results for Basic Findings of LIFT (Section 3) ‣ Appendix D Detailed and Extended Results of Experiments in the Main Paper ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref})
but cannot tolerate transferred adversarial attack, implying that the
adversarial attack on LeNet-5 is transferred to [LIFT]{#S3.SS5.p3.7.5
.ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps}/GPT-3.
:::
:::

::: {#S3.SS6 .section .ltx_subsection}
### [3.6 ]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_subsection}Does LIFT Need Large-Scale Models Pretrained on Natural Language Data? {#does-lift-need-large-scale-models-pretrained-on-natural-language-data .ltx_title .ltx_title_subsection}

::: {#S3.SS6.p1 .ltx_para}
We investigate the requirement of pretrained LMs for which
[LIFT]{#S3.SS6.p1.1.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} performs well. We
compare variants of [LIFT]{#S3.SS6.p1.1.2 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps}
under different types of LMs: GPTs pretrained on natural language data
(our models), a large LM without pretraining (Rand-GPT-J), and LMs
pre-trained on non-human language data, including
CodeParrot \[[53](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib53){.ltx_ref}\]
and
CodeGen-2B-mono \[[54](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib54){.ltx_ref}\]
trained mainly on programming language data, and a GPT-J fine-tuned on
Gibberish
data \[[55](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib55){.ltx_ref}\].
See Appendix [[D.1.5]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A4.SS1.SSS5 "D.1.5 Does LIFT Need Large-Scale Models Pretrained on Natural Language Data? ‣ D.1 Results for Basic Findings of LIFT (Section 3) ‣ Appendix D Detailed and Extended Results of Experiments in the Main Paper ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}
for the detailed setting.
:::

::: {#S3.SS6.p2 .ltx_para}
[Does LIFT only need a large pretrained model? ]{#S3.SS6.p2.1.1
.ltx_text .ltx_font_bold} To answer this question, we compare
performances of [LIFT]{#S3.SS6.p2.1.2 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps}
when GPTs are pretrained ([LIFT]{#S3.SS6.p2.1.3 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps}/GPTs) and when GPT-J have weights being randomly
initialized ([LIFT]{#S3.SS6.p2.1.4 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps}/Rand-GPT-J). More specifically, for
[LIFT]{#S3.SS6.p2.1.5 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps}/Rand-GPT-J, we
randomly initialized a GPT-J model and fine-tuned the whole model
(instead of LoRA). As shown in Table [[5]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#S3.T5 "Table 5 ‣ 3.6 Does LIFT Need Large-Scale Models Pretrained on Natural Language Data? ‣ 3 Basic Findings of LIFT ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref},
accuracies of [LIFT]{#S3.SS6.p2.1.6 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps}/Rand-GPT-J are much lower than those of our models
([LIFT]{#S3.SS6.p2.1.7 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps}/GPTs), across all
datasets. These results indicate that [LIFT]{#S3.SS6.p2.1.8 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps} benefits from pretraining, not just from the
large-scale design of LMs.
:::

::: {#S3.SS6.p3 .ltx_para}
[Does LIFT need a model trained on natural language
data? ]{#S3.SS6.p3.1.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_bold} As shown in
Table [[5]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#S3.T5 "Table 5 ‣ 3.6 Does LIFT Need Large-Scale Models Pretrained on Natural Language Data? ‣ 3 Basic Findings of LIFT ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref},
[LIFT]{#S3.SS6.p3.1.2 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps}/GPTs perform much
better than [LIFT]{#S3.SS6.p3.1.3 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps}/CodeGen
and [LIFT]{#S3.SS6.p3.1.4 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps}/CodeParrot for
all datasets. This implies that [LIFT]{#S3.SS6.p3.1.5 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps} may perform better with LMs pretrained on natural
language data. When the pretrained GPT-J is fine-tuned on gibberish
data \[[55](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib55){.ltx_ref}\],
the accuracies drop for a few tasks and are lower than
[LIFT]{#S3.SS6.p3.1.6 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps}/GPTs overall.
However, [LIFT]{#S3.SS6.p3.1.7 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps}/Gibberish
still achieves comparably good performance and its small performance
gaps to [LIFT]{#S3.SS6.p3.1.8 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps}/GPT-J can
be attributed to the relatively light impact of fine-tuning on large
pretrained LMs. Thus pretraining on natural language data is necessary
for [LIFT]{#S3.SS6.p3.1.9 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps}.
:::

<figure id="S3.T5" class="ltx_table">
<div id="S3.T5.38"
class="ltx_inline-block ltx_align_center ltx_transformed_outer"
style="width:433.6pt;height:104.4pt;vertical-align:-0.8pt;">
<span class="ltx_transformed_inner"
style="transform:translate(-46.9pt,11.2pt) scale(0.822135110954038,0.822135110954038) ;">
</span>
</div>
<figcaption><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_table"><span id="S3.T5.46.2.1"
class="ltx_text" style="font-size:90%;">Table 5</span>: </span><span
id="S3.T5.2.1" class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold"
style="font-size:90%;">Accuracies (<span class="math inline">↑</span>)
of LIFT with different LMs. <span id="S3.T5.2.1.1"
class="ltx_text ltx_font_medium"> We compare variants of <span
id="S3.T5.2.1.1.1" class="ltx_text ltx_font_smallcaps">LIFT</span> with
different LMs: <span id="S3.T5.2.1.1.2"
class="ltx_text ltx_font_smallcaps">LIFT</span>/GPTs using GPTs
pretrained on natural language data (our models), <span

fine-tuned on gibberish data. </span></span></figcaption>
</figure>
:::
:::

::: {#S4 .section .ltx_section}
## [4 ]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_section}Evaluation of [LIFT]{#S4.1.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps}-Specific Learning Properties {#evaluation-of-lift-specific-learning-properties .ltx_title .ltx_title_section}

::: {#S4.p1 .ltx_para}
In this section, we study the behavior of [LIFT]{#S4.p1.1.1 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps} in a more fine-grained manner.
:::

::: {#S4.SS1 .section .ltx_subsection}
### [4.1 ]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_subsection}Does [LIFT]{#S4.SS1.1.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} Benefit from Incorporating Feature Names? {#does-lift-benefit-from-incorporating-feature-names .ltx_title .ltx_title_subsection}

::: {#S4.SS1.p1 .ltx_para}
Unlike standard machine learning algorithms, [LIFT]{#S4.SS1.p1.1.1
.ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} can be provided context information by
incorporating the feature names and task descriptions in the prompts.
Intuitively, this incorporation may improve the sample complexity of
[LIFT]{#S4.SS1.p1.1.2 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} as the prior
knowledge already learned in the pretraining phase may help
[LIFT]{#S4.SS1.p1.1.3 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} predict better. We
design seven prompt templates to assess how incorporating feature names
affects the performance of [LIFT]{#S4.SS1.p1.1.4 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps} (see more details in Appendix [[D.2.1]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A4.SS2.SSS1 "D.2.1 Does LIFT Benefit from Incorporating Feature Names? ‣ D.2 Results for LIFT-Specific Learning Properties (Section 4) ‣ Appendix D Detailed and Extended Results of Experiments in the Main Paper ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}).
We empirically verify this intuition and show our results in
Table [[6]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#S4.T6 "Table 6 ‣ 4.1 Does LIFT Benefit from Incorporating Feature Names? ‣ 4 Evaluation of LIFT-Specific Learning Properties ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}
for several classification tasks using pretrained GPT-3 models. We first
observe that all [LIFT]{#S4.SS1.p1.1.5 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps}
models outperform MCC with significant accuracy gaps, indicating that
they are all properly trained. Second, we observe that correctly
incorporating feature names helps boost the performances of
[LIFT]{#S4.SS1.p1.1.6 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} for datasets except
for [CMC]{#S4.SS1.p1.1.7 .ltx_text .ltx_font_typewriter}. Third, if we
use similar prompts with shuffled feature names ([Shuffled-Names I,
II]{#S4.SS1.p1.1.8 .ltx_text .ltx_font_typewriter}), then the
performance of [LIFT]{#S4.SS1.p1.1.9 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps}
drops by a significant margin. These results imply that the
aforementioned performance improvements are indeed due to proper
prompting with correct feature/value association.
:::

<figure id="S4.T6" class="ltx_table">
<div id="S4.T6.25"
class="ltx_inline-block ltx_align_center ltx_transformed_outer"

<span class="ltx_transformed_inner"

</span>
</div>

</span></span></figcaption>
</figure>
:::

::: {#S4.SS2 .section .ltx_subsection}
### [4.2 ]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_subsection}Is [LIFT]{#S4.SS2.1.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} Well Calibrated? {#is-lift-well-calibrated .ltx_title .ltx_title_subsection}

::: {#S4.SS2.p1 .ltx_para}
We investigate whether [LIFT]{#S4.SS2.p1.8.1 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps} is [calibrated]{#S4.SS2.p1.8.2 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_italic}, [i.e.]{#S4.SS2.p1.8.3 .ltx_text .ltx_font_italic},
the prediction reflects the confidence, by exploring how
[LIFT]{#S4.SS2.p1.8.4 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} performs under
various noise levels, as shown in Fig. [[16]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A4.F16 "Figure 16 ‣ D.2.2 Is LIFT Calibrated? ‣ D.2 Results for LIFT-Specific Learning Properties (Section 4) ‣ Appendix D Detailed and Extended Results of Experiments in the Main Paper ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}
in Appendix. We conduct experiments on six synthetic regression
datasets, each consisting of 1,000 noisy training samples shown as blue
markers in the first row. To be specific, we generate (1) the input $x$
following the guideline in Sec. [[C.1]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A3.SS1 "C.1 Datasets ‣ Appendix C Experimental Setup ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}
for regression tasks and (2) the noisy outcome $y$ where the standard
deviation of noise ${\sigma\operatorname{}{(x)}} = {{({x + 10})}/10}$
increases along the $x$-axis (from $x = {- 10}$ to $x = 10$), and study
how different noise level affects the predictive behavior of
[LIFT]{#S4.SS2.p1.8.5 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps}. In the inference
phase, we set the decoding temperature $T = 1$ for [LIFT]{#S4.SS2.p1.8.6
.ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} to make random predictions. For
visualization purposes, we generate an additional 103 samples uniformly
in $\lbrack{- 10},10\rbrack$ for each task and plot the standard
deviation of 20 [LIFT]{#S4.SS2.p1.8.7 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps}/GPT-J predictions on each sample in the bottom row
of Fig. [[16]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A4.F16 "Figure 16 ‣ D.2.2 Is LIFT Calibrated? ‣ D.2 Results for LIFT-Specific Learning Properties (Section 4) ‣ Appendix D Detailed and Extended Results of Experiments in the Main Paper ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}.
Note that the bottom row of Fig. [[16]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A4.F16 "Figure 16 ‣ D.2.2 Is LIFT Calibrated? ‣ D.2 Results for LIFT-Specific Learning Properties (Section 4) ‣ Appendix D Detailed and Extended Results of Experiments in the Main Paper ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}
shows that the standard deviation of [LIFT]{#S4.SS2.p1.8.8 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps}/GPT-J's prediction nearly matches that of noisy
training samples (observations) across different tasks. These results
imply that [LIFT]{#S4.SS2.p1.8.9 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps}/GPT-J is
calibrated. Similarly, Fig. [[17]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A4.F17 "Figure 17 ‣ D.2.2 Is LIFT Calibrated? ‣ D.2 Results for LIFT-Specific Learning Properties (Section 4) ‣ Appendix D Detailed and Extended Results of Experiments in the Main Paper ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}
of Sec. [[D.2.2]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A4.SS2.SSS2 "D.2.2 Is LIFT Calibrated? ‣ D.2 Results for LIFT-Specific Learning Properties (Section 4) ‣ Appendix D Detailed and Extended Results of Experiments in the Main Paper ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}
shows that [LIFT]{#S4.SS2.p1.8.10 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps}/GPT-3
is calibrated.
:::

<figure id="S4.F4" class="ltx_figure ltx_align_floatright">
<div class="ltx_flex_figure">
