::: {#S4.SS3 .section .ltx_subsection}
### [4.3 ]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_subsection}Can We Use [LIFT]{#S4.SS3.1.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} for Data Generation? {#can-we-use-lift-for-data-generation .ltx_title .ltx_title_subsection}

::: {#S4.SS3.p1 .ltx_para}
Generative models have been widely used in computer
vision \[[56](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib56){.ltx_ref},
[57](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib57){.ltx_ref},
[58](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib58){.ltx_ref}\].
Beyond classification and regression tasks, we study whether
[LIFT]{#S4.SS3.p1.1.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} can be used for
generative tasks, [i.e.]{#S4.SS3.p1.1.2 .ltx_text .ltx_font_italic},
learning the underlying data distribution and generating realistic data
samples. In particular, we consider two image generation tasks on MNIST
dataset: (a) generating an image given a digit number, and (b)
completing an image given a digit number and its pixels on the top half
of the image. Fig. [[4]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#S4.F4 "Figure 4 ‣ 4.2 Is LIFT Well Calibrated? ‣ 4 Evaluation of LIFT-Specific Learning Properties ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}a
and Fig. [[4]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#S4.F4 "Figure 4 ‣ 4.2 Is LIFT Well Calibrated? ‣ 4 Evaluation of LIFT-Specific Learning Properties ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}b
show our generated images for the two tasks respectively. We observe
that the generated images have the correct digit shape and reasonably
high quality in most cases, especially for the image completion
(Fig. [[4]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#S4.F4 "Figure 4 ‣ 4.2 Is LIFT Well Calibrated? ‣ 4 Evaluation of LIFT-Specific Learning Properties ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}b).
See Appendix [[D.2.3]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A4.SS2.SSS3 "D.2.3 Can we use LIFT for Generation? ‣ D.2 Results for LIFT-Specific Learning Properties (Section 4) ‣ Appendix D Detailed and Extended Results of Experiments in the Main Paper ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}
for more details.
:::
:::
:::

::: {#S5 .section .ltx_section}
## [5 ]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_section}Improving [LIFT]{#S5.1.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} with Existing Techniques {#improving-lift-with-existing-techniques .ltx_title .ltx_title_section}

::: {#S5.p1 .ltx_para}
We improve [LIFT]{#S5.p1.1.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} with
advanced techniques: two-stage fine-tuning and data augmentation.
:::

::: {#S5.SS1 .section .ltx_subsection}
### [5.1 ]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_subsection}Two-Stage Fine-Tuning for [LIFT]{#S5.SS1.1.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} with Synthetic Pretext Tasks {#two-stage-fine-tuning-for-lift-with-synthetic-pretext-tasks .ltx_title .ltx_title_subsection}

<figure id="S5.F5" class="ltx_figure ltx_align_floatright">
<img src="./2206.06565_files/x2.png" id="S5.F5.g1"
class="ltx_graphics ltx_centering ltx_img_landscape" width="415"
height="148" alt="Refer to caption" />
<figcaption><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_figure"><span id="S5.F5.4.1.1"
class="ltx_text" style="font-size:90%;">Figure 5</span>: </span><span
id="S5.F5.5.2" class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold"
style="font-size:90%;">Two-stage fine-tuning.<span id="S5.F5.5.2.1"
class="ltx_text ltx_font_medium"> The two-stage method (blue) applies
<span id="S5.F5.5.2.1.1" class="ltx_text ltx_font_smallcaps">LIFT</span>
first on synthetic pretext data before the real datasets, outperforming
fine-tuning (green) when training data is small. The full experiment
results are presented in Fig. <a
href="https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A4.F19"
class="ltx_ref"
title="Figure 19 ‣ D.3.1 Two-Stage Intermediate Fine-Tuning for LIFT ‣ D.3 Results for Improving Techniques of LIFT (Section 5) ‣ Appendix D Detailed and Extended Results of Experiments in the Main Paper ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"><span
class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">19</span></a>. </span></span></figcaption>
</figure>

::: {#S5.SS1.p1 .ltx_para}
In Sec. [[3.2]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#S3.SS2 "3.2 How Many Samples Does LIFT Need? ‣ 3 Basic Findings of LIFT ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref},
we observe that LMs need a sufficient number of samples to start
adapting. We suspect that LMs' adaptation to non-language tasks contains
two phases: (1) learn the task description, [i.e.,]{#S5.SS1.p1.1.1
.ltx_text .ltx_font_italic} input space, label space, and sentence
templates \[[47](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib47){.ltx_ref},
[59](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib59){.ltx_ref}\],
and (2) learn the target task. Thus, we consider utilizing synthetic
data to describe the task for LMs in the first phase, thus reducing the
sample complexity. This results in a new two-stage training procedure
for [LIFT]{#S5.SS1.p1.1.2 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps}.[^1^[[^1^[1]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_note}The recent
work \[[60](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib60){.ltx_ref}\]
demonstrates the usefulness of the intermediate fine-tuning method for
LMs. However, they focus on self-supervised objectives for fine-tuning
pretrained LMs for few-shot in-context
learning.]{.ltx_note_content}]{.ltx_note_outer}]{#footnote1a .ltx_note
.ltx_role_footnote} In particular, for any given dataset, we first
generate two pretext tasks with simple synthetic Gaussian datasets
(discussed in [[C.1]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A3.SS1 "C.1 Datasets ‣ Appendix C Experimental Setup ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref})
sharing the same number of features and the label space (for
classification tasks) or the range of responses' values (for the
regression tasks) to the actual data. We apply [LIFT]{#S5.SS1.p1.1.3
.ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} on pretext tasks for a few (2 or 3)
epochs, then continue [LIFT]{#S5.SS1.p1.1.4 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps} with the target (given) dataset. For GPT-3, it is
unclear how to keep the order of samples not shuffled with the current
black-box API during the fine-tuning stage. Hence, we only provide the
experimental results of GPT-J. Fig. [[5]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#S5.F5 "Figure 5 ‣ 5.1 Two-Stage Fine-Tuning for LIFT with Synthetic Pretext Tasks ‣ 5 Improving LIFT with Existing Techniques ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}
shows that two-stage fine-tuning improves [LIFT]{#S5.SS1.p1.1.5
.ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} over the original fine-tuning when the
number of training samples is small on both classification and
regression tasks.
:::
:::

::: {#S5.SS2 .section .ltx_subsection}
### [5.2 ]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_subsection}Data Augmentation {#data-augmentation .ltx_title .ltx_title_subsection}


::: {#S5.SS2.p1 .ltx_para}
Data
augmentation \[[61](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib61){.ltx_ref}\]
is a simple tool for improving the generalization performance for
various classification problems. Here, we investigate whether data
augmentation benefits [LIFT]{#S5.SS2.p1.2.1 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps}. Table [[7]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#S5.T7 "Table 7 ‣ 5.2 Data Augmentation ‣ 5 Improving LIFT with Existing Techniques ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}
shows the effect of adding random noise in the training data on the
performance of [LIFT]{#S5.SS2.p1.2.2 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps}/GPT-J for the MNIST classification problem. Here,
we test each model on three settings: (1) clean data, (2) Gaussian
noise, and (3) signed constant noise. We allow each noise can perturb up
to the magnitude of $\epsilon \in {\lbrack 0,1\rbrack}$ at each
dimension ([i.e.]{#S5.SS2.p1.2.3 .ltx_text .ltx_font_italic}, each
pixel) when the black/white pixel of MNIST is represented in the
$\lbrack 0,1\rbrack$ range. We defer the generation procedure of random
Gaussian noise to Sec. [[D.1.4]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A4.SS1.SSS4.Px4 "Robustness to feature corruption on test data. ‣ D.1.4 How Robust Is LIFT? ‣ D.1 Results for Basic Findings of LIFT (Section 3) ‣ Appendix D Detailed and Extended Results of Experiments in the Main Paper ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}
in Appendix.
:::

::: {#S5.SS2.p2 .ltx_para}
One can observe that [LIFT]{#S5.SS2.p2.1.1 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps}/GPT-J without any data augmentation (DA) is
vulnerable to random noise, unlike existing baselines (LeNet-5 and MLP).
However, when we apply data augmentation, [i.e.]{#S5.SS2.p2.1.2
.ltx_text .ltx_font_italic}, train [LIFT]{#S5.SS2.p2.1.3 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps}/GPT-J with noisy training data, the accuracy
improves significantly for the perturbed (either adding Gaussian noise
or Signed constant noise) test data. This shows the effectiveness of
simple data augmentation in [LIFT]{#S5.SS2.p2.1.4 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps}. Exploring the effect of other data augmentation
schemes, [e.g.]{#S5.SS2.p2.1.5 .ltx_text .ltx_font_italic},
mixup \[[62](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib62){.ltx_ref}\]
and its
variants \[[63](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib63){.ltx_ref},
[64](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib64){.ltx_ref},
[65](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib65){.ltx_ref}\],
is remained an interesting future work.
:::
:::
:::

::: {#S6 .section .ltx_section}
## [6 ]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_section}Related Works {#related-works .ltx_title .ltx_title_section}

::: {#S6.p1 .ltx_para}
[Fine-tuning for adapting LMs to non-language tasks. ]{#S6.p1.1.1
.ltx_text .ltx_font_bold}
Fine-tuning \[[66](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib66){.ltx_ref}\]
pretrained LMs is the standard practice for learning downstream tasks,
which may involve simple architecture modifications, such as adding
linear
layers \[[67](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib67){.ltx_ref},
[68](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib68){.ltx_ref}\]
or freezing
layers \[[18](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib18){.ltx_ref},
[69](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib69){.ltx_ref},
[70](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib70){.ltx_ref}\].
The recent progress focuses on *parameter-efficient* techniques for
reducing trainable parameters, including adapter-based
fine-tuning \[[25](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib25){.ltx_ref},
[26](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib26){.ltx_ref},
[27](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib27){.ltx_ref}\]
that trains additional small residual blocks between layers,
freezing-based
fine-tuning \[[71](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib71){.ltx_ref},
[18](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib18){.ltx_ref},
[72](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib72){.ltx_ref}\]
that freezes most of the pretrained parameters, and distillation-based
fine-tuning \[[73](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib73){.ltx_ref}\].
Our [LIFT]{#S6.p1.1.3 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps}/GPT-J is fine-tuned
with
LoRA \[[24](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib24){.ltx_ref}\],
a parameter-efficient method approximating the weight updates using
low-rank matrices.
:::

::: {#S6.p2 .ltx_para}
To directly adopt existing fine-tuning methods of LMs for non-language
tasks, it is common practice to modify the input/output layers and the
loss functions, which may cause undesired behaviors like catastrophic
forgetting \[[66](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib66){.ltx_ref},
[74](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib74){.ltx_ref}\].
Our work is highly motivated by Frozen Pretrained Transformer
(FPT) \[[18](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib18){.ltx_ref}\]
that directly fine-tunes
GPT-2 \[[21](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib21){.ltx_ref}\]
pretrained on language tasks for other modalities by freezing most
pretrained parameters and adding only input and output layers for the
modality adaptation. Unlike FPT, our method requires no such changes in
the architecture and objective function. Several works also extend the
existing LMs to handle different input data types, such as
images \[[75](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib75){.ltx_ref},
[76](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib76){.ltx_ref}\],
audio \[[77](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib77){.ltx_ref}\],
tabular
data \[[78](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib78){.ltx_ref}\],
and knowledge
base \[[79](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib79){.ltx_ref}\]
by updating the pretraining phase with these data and their
corresponding tasks or using general-purpose
architecture \[[80](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib80){.ltx_ref}\].
Our work is based on GPT language models trained *only* on textual data.
:::

::: {#S6.p3 .ltx_para}
[Analyzing the adaptability of LMs. ]{#S6.p3.1.1 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_bold} Similar to ours, recent
works \[[81](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib81){.ltx_ref},
[82](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib82){.ltx_ref},
[83](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib83){.ltx_ref},
[82](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib82){.ltx_ref},
[84](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib84){.ltx_ref}\]
attempt to understand and quantify the
adaptability \[[20](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib20){.ltx_ref}\]
and capacity of large LMs, such as
Big-Bench \[[82](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib82){.ltx_ref}\]
with a new benchmark of more than 200 tasks on a diverse set of topics.
:::

::: {#S6.p4 .ltx_para}
[General-purpose models. ]{#S6.p4.1.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_bold} A
primary goal of our work is to push the limit of the existing generalist
language models ([e.g.,]{#S6.p4.1.2 .ltx_text .ltx_font_italic}
GPT-3 \[[19](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib19){.ltx_ref}\])
to other modalities and domains, supporting the idea of building a
domain-and-modality agnostic generalist
model \[[19](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib19){.ltx_ref},
[85](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib85){.ltx_ref},
[3](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib3){.ltx_ref},
[86](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib86){.ltx_ref},
[87](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib87){.ltx_ref},
[88](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib88){.ltx_ref},
[89](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib89){.ltx_ref},
[90](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib90){.ltx_ref}\].
Note that [LIFT]{#S6.p4.1.3 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} can be
applied to any generalist model with LM-like architectures, such as
GATO \[[90](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib90){.ltx_ref}\].
Furthermore, our work shares the general goal with automated machine
learning
(AutoML) \[[91](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib91){.ltx_ref},
[92](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib92){.ltx_ref}\]
in improving the usability of machine learning, though [LIFT]{#S6.p4.1.4
.ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} uses only a single pretrained LMs for all
tasks while AutoML automates the standard machine learning pipeline from
a set of existing algorithms.
:::
:::

::: {#S7 .section .ltx_section}
## [7 ]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_section}Discussion and Conclusion {#discussion-and-conclusion .ltx_title .ltx_title_section}

::: {#S7.p1 .ltx_para}
We propose the use of [language-interfaced]{#S7.p1.1.1 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_bold} framework, via [Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning
([LIFT]{#S7.p1.1.2.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps})]{#S7.p1.1.2
.ltx_text .ltx_font_bold}, for using LMs to solve non-language
downstream tasks without changing the models' architecture or loss
function. [LIFT]{#S7.p1.1.3 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} first
converts labeled samples into sentences and then fine-tunes pretrained
LMs on the sentence dataset using the standard fine-tuning method and
loss function. Via an extensive empirical study, we show that
[LIFT]{#S7.p1.1.4 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps}/GPT performs relatively
well on low-dimensional classification and regression non-language
tasks. Furthermore, [LIFT]{#S7.p1.1.5 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps}/GPTs are robust in several practical settings, and
can properly calibrate the predictions and generate realistic data
samples. [LIFT]{#S7.p1.1.6 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} can be
improved using in-context feature names, two-stage fine-tuning, and data
augmentation. Moreover, our work is arguably one of the [first to
thoroughly study the efficacy of language-interfaced learning
framework]{#S7.p1.1.7 .ltx_text .ltx_font_italic} with pretrained
language models on standard regression and classification tasks, paving
the way for enabling "no-code machine learning with language models."
:::

::: {#S7.p2 .ltx_para}
[Limitations and open questions. ]{#S7.p2.1.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_bold}
Despite promising performances on various tasks and settings, we observe
some limitations of [LIFT]{#S7.p2.1.2 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} to
basic learning tasks. [LIFT]{#S7.p2.1.3 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps}/GPT do not perform well if the features have high
dimensions (for regression) or when the number of classes is large (for
classification). In addition, the context length of [LIFT]{#S7.p2.1.4
.ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} is restricted to the context length of
LMs and [LIFT]{#S7.p2.1.5 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps}/GPT is
memory-inefficient. One can combine [LIFT]{#S7.p2.1.6 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps} with memory-efficient LMs such as
LinTransformer \[[93](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib93){.ltx_ref}\]
to address this issue. Besides, our works open some interesting
questions for future works. First, do LMs and [LIFT]{#S7.p2.1.7
.ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps}/GPT have behaviors similar to ensemble
methods or decision tree since they have similar decision boundaries?
Secondly, are LMs universal models that can adapt well to any modalities
and domains? Lastly, can [LIFT]{#S7.p2.1.8 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps}/GPTs adapt better for regression tasks using more
sophisticated encoding schemes for numeric features?
:::

::: {#S7.p3 .ltx_para}
[Social impacts. ]{#S7.p3.1.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_bold} Future research
should also investigate potential fairness issues of applying
[LIFT]{#S7.p3.1.2 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps}. Based on large
language models, [LIFT]{#S7.p3.1.3 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} might
have embedded bias targeting certain social groups. Especially when
feature names are included in the training prompts, the models may be
more sensitive to social biases and thus might make unfair and harmful
predictions. We leave measuring the embedded bias in LIFT as one of the
interesting future directions.
:::

::: {#Sx2 .section .ltx_section}
## Checklist {#checklist .ltx_title .ltx_title_section}

::: {#Sx2.p1 .ltx_para}
1.  [[1.]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_item}]{#Sx2.I1.i1}

    ::: {#Sx2.I1.i1.p1 .ltx_para}
    For all authors...

    1.  [[(a)]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_item}]{#Sx2.I1.i1.I1.i1}

        ::: {#Sx2.I1.i1.I1.i1.p1 .ltx_para}
        Do the main claims made in the abstract and introduction
        accurately reflect the paper's contributions and scope?
        [\[Yes\]]{#Sx2.I1.i1.I1.i1.p1.1.1 .ltx_text
        style="color:#0000FF;"}
        :::

    The contribution and scope of this paper has been summarized in the
    abstract and the last part of Introduction.
    :::
2.  [[2.]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_item}]{#Sx2.I1.i2}

    ::: {#Sx2.I1.i2.p1 .ltx_para}
    Did you describe the limitations of your work?
    [\[Yes\]]{#Sx2.I1.i2.p1.1.1 .ltx_text style="color:#0000FF;"}
    :::

We wrote the limitations in Sec.7.
:::

::: {#Sx2.p2 .ltx_para}
Did you discuss any potential negative societal impacts of your work?
[\[Yes\] ]{#Sx2.p2.1.1 .ltx_text style="color:#0000FF;"}This has been
discussed in Sec.7. Have you read the ethics review guidelines and
ensured that your paper conforms to them? [\[Yes\] ]{#Sx2.p2.1.2
.ltx_text style="color:#0000FF;"}Our paper conforms to the ethics
guideline. If you are including theoretical results...

1.  [[1.]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_item}]{#Sx2.I2.i1}

    ::: {#Sx2.I2.i1.p1 .ltx_para}
    Did you state the full set of assumptions of all theoretical
    results? [\[N/A\] ]{#Sx2.I2.i1.p1.1.1 .ltx_text
    style="color:#808080;"}
    :::
2.  [[2.]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_item}]{#Sx2.I2.i2}

    ::: {#Sx2.I2.i2.p1 .ltx_para}
    Did you include complete proofs of all theoretical results? [\[N/A\]
    ]{#Sx2.I2.i2.p1.1.1 .ltx_text style="color:#808080;"}
    :::

[Appendix]{#Sx2.p2.5.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_bold style="font-size:120%;"}
Due to the space limit, many interesting findings, discussions, and
further details are included in Appendix. We start by discussing the
detailed motivation of our work in ([[A]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A1 "Appendix A Further Motivations of LIFT ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref})
and introducing related work in ([[B]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A2 "Appendix B Detailed Related Works ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}).
We then describe our datasets in ([[C.1]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A3.SS1 "C.1 Datasets ‣ Appendix C Experimental Setup ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref})
and the implementation of all models in ([[C.2]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A3.SS2 "C.2 LIFT and Baseline Implementation ‣ Appendix C Experimental Setup ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}).
For the experimental results, we provide detailed and extended results
for all findings presented in the main paper in ([[D]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A4 "Appendix D Detailed and Extended Results of Experiments in the Main Paper ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}),
with more visualizations, score tables, and additional evaluations on
more functions, datasets, and properties. In ([[E]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A5 "Appendix E Additional Experiments and Findings (NOT Discussed in the Main Paper) ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}),
we present additional interesting findings and visualizations which have
not been discussed so far in the main paper. Lastly, we provide a more
profound discussion in ([[F]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A6 "Appendix F Additional Discussion ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}).

::: {#A1 .section .ltx_appendix}
## [Appendix A ]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_appendix}Further Motivations of [LIFT]{#A1.1.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} {#appendix-a-further-motivations-of-lift .ltx_title .ltx_title_appendix}

::: {#A1.p1 .ltx_para}
While we mainly emphasized the "no-code-ML" property of
[LIFT]{#A1.p1.1.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps}, indeed, it has a lot
of potentials to be more useful and powerful than many of the current ML
models. Particularly, [LIFT]{#A1.p1.1.2 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps}
can bring an entirely novel approach to enable (i) explainability and
(ii) updatability via information retrieval.
:::

::: {#A1.SS1 .section .ltx_subsection}
### [A.1 ]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_subsection}Explainability {#a.1-explainability .ltx_title .ltx_title_subsection}

::: {#A1.SS1.p1 .ltx_para}
Most ML models cannot interpret their predictions. While specific
algorithms are developed to enable such models' explainability, their
efficacy is still in question. On the other hand, LMs have been shown to
be able to explain their predictions
 \[[94](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib94){.ltx_ref},
[95](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib95){.ltx_ref}\].
This ability has been used in many fields such as human moral
judgements \[[96](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib96){.ltx_ref}\]
and mathematical
reasoning \[[97](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib97){.ltx_ref}\].
Therefore, [LIFT]{#A1.SS1.p1.1.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps}, based
on a large pretrained LM, can be made to explain its prediction using
its reasoning capabilities. Consider the German-credit
dataset \[[98](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib98){.ltx_ref}\],
which aims to predict whether the bank should approve or decline loan
applications. After asking GPT-3 if one should approve/decline a loan
application via [LIFT]{#A1.SS1.p1.1.2 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps},
one can also ask GPT-3 to explain its own prediction result. This can be
implemented by making two consecutive inference calls, as shown in
Table [[8]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A1.T8 "Table 8 ‣ A.1 Explainability ‣ Appendix A Further Motivations of LIFT ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}.
Here, we provide five different responses generated with different
random seeds.
:::

<figure id="A1.T8" class="ltx_table">
<div id="A1.T8.6"
class="ltx_inline-block ltx_align_center ltx_transformed_outer"
style="width:433.6pt;height:392.7pt;vertical-align:-0.7pt;">
<span class="ltx_transformed_inner"
style="transform:translate(-90.1pt,81.5pt) scale(0.706348070671196,0.706348070671196) ;">
</span>
</div>
<figcaption><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_table"><span id="A1.T8.4.1.1"
class="ltx_text" style="font-size:90%;">Table 8</span>: </span><span
id="A1.T8.5.2" class="ltx_text" style="font-size:90%;"> <span
id="A1.T8.5.2.1" class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold">An example illustrating
the reasoning capability of large pretrained language models.</span> We
convert a data sample from German-credit dataset [<a
href="https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib98"
class="ltx_ref">98</a>] into text and use GPT-3 to predict whether the
bank should approve or decline the individual’s loan application. After
receiving the first response of “accept”, we add “The bank will accept
this loan application. Let’s think logically. This is because” to the
original prompt and use it as the new input of GPT-3. The five different
responses generated by GPT-3 with different random seeds demonstrate the
reasoning ability of language models. Note that these explanations are
generated without seeing any reasoning examples, <span id="A1.T8.5.2.2"
class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic">i.e.</span>, these are solely based on
the zero-shot reasoning capability of language models.
</span></figcaption>
</figure>

::: {#A1.SS1.p2 .ltx_para}
Note that these explanations are generated without seeing *any*
reasoning examples, [i.e.]{#A1.SS1.p2.1.2 .ltx_text .ltx_font_italic},
these are solely based on the zero-shot reasoning capability of language
models. While these explanations are not even close to perfect, they are
still reasonable. Improving the few-/zero-shot reasoning capabilities of
language models is a fast-growing research field, so incorporating new
techniques can further improve the explainability of
[LIFT]{#A1.SS1.p2.1.3 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps}.
:::
:::

::: {#A1.SS2 .section .ltx_subsection}
### [A.2 ]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_subsection}Updatability via Information Retrieval {#a.2-updatability-via-information-retrieval .ltx_title .ltx_title_subsection}

::: {#A1.SS2.p1 .ltx_para}
Another drawback of current ML models is that it is difficult to
"update" models when a distribution shift occurs. Handling these
distribution shifts in a compute-efficient manner has recently become
one of the most active research areas in the field. Recently, augmenting
LMs \[[99](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib99){.ltx_ref},
[100](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib100){.ltx_ref},
[101](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib101){.ltx_ref},
[102](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib102){.ltx_ref}\]
with a retrieval mechanism has shown to be an efficient way of updating
LMs. With such a retrieval mechanism equipped, LMs can be efficiently
updated as one can simply update its associated database, or even
connect LMs to the
Internet \[[102](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib102){.ltx_ref}\].
While we only used LMs not equipped with a retrieval mechanism in this
paper, it is straightforward to apply our framework to other LMs that
can retrieve information from databases or the Internet,
[i.e.]{#A1.SS2.p1.1.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_italic}, [LIFT]{#A1.SS2.p1.1.2
.ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} can support a compute-efficient update
mechanism.
:::
:::
:::

::: {#A2 .section .ltx_appendix}
## [Appendix B ]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_appendix}Detailed Related Works {#appendix-b-detailed-related-works .ltx_title .ltx_title_appendix}

::: {#A2.p1 .ltx_para}
This section provides detailed related work.
:::

::: {#A2.SS0.SSS0.Px1 .section .ltx_paragraph}
##### Pretraining and adapting language models (LMs). {#pretraining-and-adapting-language-models-lms. .ltx_title .ltx_title_paragraph}

::: {#A2.SS0.SSS0.Px1.p1 .ltx_para}
Our work uses modern large LMs, which promoted significant advances in
the field of natural language processing
(NLP) \[[4](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib4){.ltx_ref}\].
Most popular LMs use transformer
architectures \[[12](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib12){.ltx_ref},
[13](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib13){.ltx_ref}\]
as the backbone, from early models like
BERT \[[103](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib103){.ltx_ref}\]
built on Transformer encoders to GPT
variants \[[104](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib104){.ltx_ref},
[21](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib21){.ltx_ref}\]
built on Transformer decoders. Multiple modern large LMs have been
proposed, including
RoBERTa \[[105](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib105){.ltx_ref}\],
ALBERT \[[106](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib106){.ltx_ref}\],
XLNet \[[107](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib107){.ltx_ref}\],
and the latest models with billions or trillions of parameters, such as
GPT-3 \[[108](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib108){.ltx_ref}\],
Switch-Transformers \[[109](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib109){.ltx_ref}\],
and
PALM \[[110](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib110){.ltx_ref}\].
LMs are trained to encode a large amount of linguistic knowledge from
multiple sources in their contextual
representations \[[111](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib111){.ltx_ref}\],
which are helpful and can be easily adapted to various other tasks.
Thus, starting with
BERT \[[103](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib103){.ltx_ref}\],
it has become a standard practice to pretrain and then fine-tunes a
large LM for plenty of downstream tasks in lieu of training a model from
scratch for a specific
task \[[103](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib103){.ltx_ref},
[19](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib19){.ltx_ref},
[4](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib4){.ltx_ref},
[105](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib105){.ltx_ref},
[112](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib112){.ltx_ref}\].
This technique dramatically impacts a wide range of NLP tasks, such as
language
modeling \[[19](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib19){.ltx_ref},
[110](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib110){.ltx_ref}\],
question answering systems
 \[[113](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib113){.ltx_ref},
[114](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib114){.ltx_ref}\],
text
summarization \[[115](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib115){.ltx_ref}\],
neural machine
translation \[[116](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib116){.ltx_ref}\],
and
reasoning \[[117](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib117){.ltx_ref},
[118](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib118){.ltx_ref}\].
However, the excellent performance of fine-tuned LMs without
architecture changes has been mainly limited to NLP tasks so far. This
work, instead, investigates whether we can leverage LM fine-tuning for
non-language tasks across different modalities. Our work is highly
motivated by Frozen Pretrained Transformer
(FPT) \[[18](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib18){.ltx_ref}\],
which directly adapts
GPT-2 \[[21](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib21){.ltx_ref}\]
pretrained on language tasks and textual data to other modalities. FPT
freezes most pretrained parameters except the layer normalization layers
and adds input and output layers for fine-tuning. The authors
empirically show that GPT-2 can be efficiently fine-tuned for different
modalities and domains, including vision and numeric computation.
Nevertheless, FPT requires changes in the architecture and objective
function to adapt to different data representations, while our method
[LIFT]{#A2.SS0.SSS0.Px1.p1.1.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} does not.
Furthermore, we mainly focus on basic machine learning tasks, such as
function approximation or tabular data classification. Several works
have attempted to extend the existing LMs to handle different types of
input data, such as
images \[[75](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib75){.ltx_ref},
[76](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib76){.ltx_ref}\],
audio \[[77](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib77){.ltx_ref}\],
tabular
data \[[78](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib78){.ltx_ref}\],
and knowledge bases
 \[[79](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib79){.ltx_ref}\]
by updating the pretraining phase with these data and their
corresponding tasks. For instance,
XGPT \[[119](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib119){.ltx_ref}\]
takes images as the input and uses the image captioning task as the
primary task in the pretraining stage for GPT to generate images'
captions. Similarly, multiple works utilize pretrained LMs for
generating text descriptions of images or videos in image captioning
(VisualGPT \[[120](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib120){.ltx_ref}\])
and video captioning
(VideoBERT \[[121](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib121){.ltx_ref}\],
Unified
VLP \[[122](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib122){.ltx_ref}\],
UniVL \[[123](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib123){.ltx_ref}\]).
SpeechBERT \[[77](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib77){.ltx_ref}\]
also integrates LMs for speech recognition in the weakly-supervised
setting to reduce the need for scarce supervised data. LMs can also
adapt to numeric
tasks \[[124](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib124){.ltx_ref}\]
or other domains such as protein
folding \[[125](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib125){.ltx_ref}\]
or symbolic math
solvers \[[126](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib126){.ltx_ref}\].
Recent
works \[[127](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib127){.ltx_ref},
[128](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib128){.ltx_ref},
[78](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib78){.ltx_ref}\]
pretrain LMs with large tabular datasets, improving the question
answering systems by reasoning over the tables. Compared with these
existing works, our work is unique in that it is based on GPT language
models trained *only* on textual data.
:::
:::

::: {#A2.SS0.SSS0.Px2 .section .ltx_paragraph}
##### Analyzing the adaptability of LMs. {#analyzing-the-adaptability-of-lms. .ltx_title .ltx_title_paragraph}

::: {#A2.SS0.SSS0.Px2.p1 .ltx_para}
Similar to our work, recent
works \[[20](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib20){.ltx_ref},
[81](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib81){.ltx_ref}\]
have made efforts to understand and quantify the feasibility and
limitations of the adaptability of large LMs for upstream performance
and downstream tasks. For instance, the recent
work \[[20](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib20){.ltx_ref}\]
built a benchmark of 500 small language tasks for testing the
adaptability of LMs, observing that the
LMs \[[129](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib129){.ltx_ref}\]
can adapt well to an extensive range of complex tasks rather than just
memorizing the learned patterns.
BIG-bench \[[82](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib82){.ltx_ref}\]
is recently introduced as a new benchmark for quantifying the capacity
of LMs, consisting of more than 200 tasks on a diverse set of topics.
Another relevant
work \[[83](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib83){.ltx_ref}\]
attempts to understand the effect of LM pretraining by studying how the
transformer architecture, the backbone of LMs, succeeds at a designed
synthetic task. Similar efforts in this line of work are to analyze the
behaviors, representations, and inductive bias of pretrained
LMs \[[130](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib130){.ltx_ref},
[131](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib131){.ltx_ref},
[132](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib132){.ltx_ref}\]
or investigate different aspects of
LMs \[[133](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib133){.ltx_ref},
[134](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib134){.ltx_ref},
[135](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib135){.ltx_ref}\].
For instance, a recent
work \[[133](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib133){.ltx_ref}\],
in the investigation of the difficulty of numeracy in LMs observes that
transformer-based language models do not work well on complex numeric
tasks and are sensitive to different formats of numeracy. Note that
these existing works focus primarily on downstream language tasks, while
we focus on adapting LMs on non-language tasks without any modification
of the loss or architecture.
:::
:::

::: {#A2.SS0.SSS0.Px3 .section .ltx_paragraph}
##### Methods for adapting LMs. {#methods-for-adapting-lms. .ltx_title .ltx_title_paragraph}

::: {#A2.SS0.SSS0.Px3.p1 .ltx_para}
The most common method for adapting LMs is
[fine-tuning]{#A2.SS0.SSS0.Px3.p1.1.1 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_italic} \[[66](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib66){.ltx_ref}\]
which aims to slightly adjust pretrained parameters for learning the
downstream
tasks \[[136](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib136){.ltx_ref},
[137](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib137){.ltx_ref}\].
Fine-tuning can involve simple architecture modifications, such as
adding linear
layers \[[67](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib67){.ltx_ref},
[68](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib68){.ltx_ref}\]
or freezing parts of the
network \[[18](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib18){.ltx_ref},
[69](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib69){.ltx_ref},
[70](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib70){.ltx_ref}\].
Fine-tuning can be improved with advanced techniques, such as
multi-stage
methods \[[138](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib138){.ltx_ref}\],
intermediate
fine-tuning \[[139](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib139){.ltx_ref},
[140](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib140){.ltx_ref},
[141](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib141){.ltx_ref}\],
or self-supervised
training \[[142](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib142){.ltx_ref}\].
The recent progress in fine-tuning LMs focuses on the
*parameter-efficient* techniques for minimizing the number of
fine-tunable parameters, including adapter-based
fine-tuning \[[25](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib25){.ltx_ref},
[26](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib26){.ltx_ref},
[27](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib27){.ltx_ref}\]
that adds and trains small residual blocks between transformer layers,
freezing-based
fine-tuning \[[71](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib71){.ltx_ref},
[18](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib18){.ltx_ref},
[72](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib72){.ltx_ref}\]
that freezes most of the pretrained parameters and fine-tunes only tiny
parts of the networks, and distillation-based
fine-tuning \[[73](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib73){.ltx_ref}\].
In this line of work,
LoRA \[[24](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib24){.ltx_ref}\]
further reduces the number of trainable parameters in large LMs by
approximating the weight updates using low-rank matrices without
changing pretrained parameters. LoRA is used as the fine-tuning method
for GPT-J in our [LIFT]{#A2.SS0.SSS0.Px3.p1.1.3 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps}/GPT-J framework. To directly adopt these
fine-tuning methods used in LMs for non-language tasks, it is common
practice to modify the input/output layers and the loss functions.
However, these modifications might lead to undesired behaviors like
catastrophic
forgetting \[[66](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib66){.ltx_ref},
[74](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib74){.ltx_ref}\].
On the other hand, our method [LIFT]{#A2.SS0.SSS0.Px3.p1.1.4 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps} uses the language interface for fine-tuning without
making any changes to the architecture or the loss function. In-context
few-shot learning
paradigm \[[143](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib143){.ltx_ref},
[144](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib144){.ltx_ref},
[28](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib28){.ltx_ref},
[29](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib29){.ltx_ref},
[145](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib145){.ltx_ref}\]
suggests modifying only the inputs of LMs by adding a few examples of
the downstream task. A critical part of these methods is reformulating
the downstream task samples to the language modeling
inputs \[[47](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib47){.ltx_ref},
[59](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib59){.ltx_ref}\],
resulting in multiple efforts in
generating \[[146](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib146){.ltx_ref}\],
searching \[[48](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib48){.ltx_ref}\],
and properly tuning the
prompts \[[147](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib147){.ltx_ref},
[148](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib148){.ltx_ref},
[149](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib149){.ltx_ref}\].
While these methods have shown great effectiveness for multiple NLP
downstream tasks, it is unclear how to apply them to downstream tasks of
other modalities. On the other hand, our work successfully adapts LMs
for non-language tasks, further pushing the application boundaries of
large-scale LMs.
:::
:::

::: {#A2.SS0.SSS0.Px4 .section .ltx_paragraph}
##### Deep learning for tabular datasets. {#deep-learning-for-tabular-datasets. .ltx_title .ltx_title_paragraph}

::: {#A2.SS0.SSS0.Px4.p1 .ltx_para}
While deep neural networks have been successfully applied to various
data types, such as images or text, they still face difficulties with a
few classification and regression tasks on tabular
data \[[150](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib150){.ltx_ref},
[151](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib151){.ltx_ref}\],
one of the most popular data types in practice. This may be due to the
heterogeneous nature of tabular data, with their features being sparse,
type-mixed, and weaker in correlation than natural image-language
data \[[152](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib152){.ltx_ref},
[150](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib150){.ltx_ref}\].
Multiple deep learning methods and architectures have been proposed for
tabular datasets, from making discrete decision trees more
differentiable \[[153](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib153){.ltx_ref},
[154](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib154){.ltx_ref}\],
regularizing neural networks'
weights \[[155](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib155){.ltx_ref},
[156](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib156){.ltx_ref}\],
to recent attempts using attention-based
modules \[[157](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib157){.ltx_ref},
[158](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib158){.ltx_ref},
[159](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib159){.ltx_ref}\].
Though these transformer-based models are the closest works to us in
this line of work, their works focus on designing and improving
architecture designs for specifically learning the tabular data rather
than adapting the LMs. To the best of our knowledge, we are the first to
thoroughly study large LM adaptation for tabular learning without
architecture changes. Our work shows promising results of LMs in closing
the gap to the best-performing methods, including tree-based ensemble
algorithms (Random
Forest \[[160](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib160){.ltx_ref}\]
or
XGBoost \[[161](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib161){.ltx_ref}\]).
:::
:::

::: {#A2.SS0.SSS0.Px5 .section .ltx_paragraph}
##### General-purpose models (generalist models). {#general-purpose-models-generalist-models. .ltx_title .ltx_title_paragraph}

::: {#A2.SS0.SSS0.Px5.p1 .ltx_para}
A primary goal of our work is to push the limit of the existing
generalist language models ([e.g.,]{#A2.SS0.SSS0.Px5.p1.1.1 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_italic}
GPT-3 \[[19](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib19){.ltx_ref}\])
to other modalities and domains, supporting the idea of building a
domain-and-modality agnostic generalist model. Early
works \[[162](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib162){.ltx_ref},
[14](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib14){.ltx_ref},
[163](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib163){.ltx_ref}\]
explored this idea by developing and training multi-task and multi-modal
models on a wide range of diverse tasks to obtain better generalization
and adaptation. The development of large-scale LMs has significantly
contributed to the area of generalist models for
languages \[[19](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib19){.ltx_ref},
[85](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib85){.ltx_ref},
[3](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib3){.ltx_ref}\],
vision \[[86](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib86){.ltx_ref}\],
visual
language \[[87](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib87){.ltx_ref},
[88](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib88){.ltx_ref},
[89](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib89){.ltx_ref}\],
and control
problems \[[90](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib90){.ltx_ref}\].
These generalist models are usually trained with the scale on an
extensive range of corpora, probably containing multiple modalities and
domains. In this line of work, a general-purpose
architecture \[[80](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib80){.ltx_ref}\]
has also been studied to handle different input and output data types.
Moreover, a recent work proposes a new language model as a
general-purpose interface to connect multiple pretrained foundation
models. This new language model is shown to work well on both
language-only and vision-language
tasks \[[84](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib84){.ltx_ref}\].
Although [LIFT]{#A2.SS0.SSS0.Px5.p1.1.2 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps}
primarily focuses on the LMs, it can be applied to other generalist
models with LM-like architectures, such as
GATO \[[90](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib90){.ltx_ref}\].
Furthermore, it is worth noticing that our work shares the general goal
with automated machine learning
(AutoML) \[[91](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib91){.ltx_ref},
[92](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib92){.ltx_ref}\]
in improving the usability of machine learning. AutoML automates the
standard machine learning pipeline for model selection and
hyperparameter tuning from a set of existing algorithms. At the same
time, [LIFT]{#A2.SS0.SSS0.Px5.p1.1.3 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} uses
only a single pretrained LM for solving all tasks.
:::
:::
:::

::: {#A3 .section .ltx_appendix}
## [Appendix C ]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_appendix}Experimental Setup {#appendix-c-experimental-setup .ltx_title .ltx_title_appendix}

::: {#A3.SS1 .section .ltx_subsection}
### [C.1 ]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_subsection}Datasets {#c.1-datasets .ltx_title .ltx_title_subsection}

::: {#A3.SS1.SSS0.Px1 .section .ltx_paragraph}
##### Classification datasets. {#classification-datasets. .ltx_title .ltx_title_paragraph}

<figure id="A3.F6" class="ltx_figure">
<img src="./2206.06565_files/x3.png" id="A3.F6.g1"
class="ltx_graphics ltx_centering ltx_img_landscape" width="664"
height="207" alt="Refer to caption" />
<figcaption><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_figure"><span id="A3.F6.3.1.1"
class="ltx_text" style="font-size:90%;">Figure 6</span>: </span><span
id="A3.F6.4.2" class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold"
style="font-size:90%;">Rolls dataset of 2, 3, 5 classes with 300 samples
per dataset (all classes are balanced). <span id="A3.F6.4.2.1"
class="ltx_text ltx_font_medium"> </span></span></figcaption>
</figure>

::: {#A3.SS1.SSS0.Px1.p1 .ltx_para}
Table [[9]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A3.T9 "Table 9 ‣ Regression datasets. ‣ C.1 Datasets ‣ Appendix C Experimental Setup ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}
summarizes the datasets used for classification tasks. We use two
additional types of synthetic datasets: [neural-net-based
datasets]{#A3.SS1.SSS0.Px1.p1.4.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_bold} used for
understanding the inductive biases of algorithms (in
Sec. [[3.4]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#S3.SS4 "3.4 Can We Understand the Inductive Biases of Language Models via LIFT? ‣ 3 Basic Findings of LIFT ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}
and Appendix [[D.1.3]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A4.SS1.SSS3 "D.1.3 Can We Understand the Inductive Biases of Language Models via LIFT? ‣ D.1 Results for Basic Findings of LIFT (Section 3) ‣ Appendix D Detailed and Extended Results of Experiments in the Main Paper ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref})
and [Gaussian pretext datasets]{#A3.SS1.SSS0.Px1.p1.4.2 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_bold} for the two-stage fine-tuning experiments (in
Sec. [[5.1]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#S5.SS1 "5.1 Two-Stage Fine-Tuning for LIFT with Synthetic Pretext Tasks ‣ 5 Improving LIFT with Existing Techniques ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}).
The [neural-net-based datasets]{#A3.SS1.SSS0.Px1.p1.4.3 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_italic} are generated as follows. For binary classification,
we train a 2-layer neural network with [tanh]{#A3.SS1.SSS0.Px1.p1.4.4
.ltx_text .ltx_font_typewriter} activation functions using the
[Rolls]{#A3.SS1.SSS0.Px1.p1.4.5 .ltx_text .ltx_font_italic} dataset
shown in the leftmost figure in Fig. [[6]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A3.F6 "Figure 6 ‣ Classification datasets. ‣ C.1 Datasets ‣ Appendix C Experimental Setup ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref},
and take six snapshots of the decision boundary of the trained neural
network shown in Fig. [[7]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A3.F7 "Figure 7 ‣ Classification datasets. ‣ C.1 Datasets ‣ Appendix C Experimental Setup ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref};
we took snapshots at training epochs 10, 40, 80, 210, 320 and 490. Then,
for each snapshot, we define a synthetic dataset (what we call a
neural-net-based dataset) having labels as the neural network's
prediction for randomly chosen 2000 samples. For 3-class and 5-class
classifications, we also use 2000 samples to train a 2-layer neural
network using the [Rolls]{#A3.SS1.SSS0.Px1.p1.4.6 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_italic} dataset shown as the second and the third figure in
Fig. [[6]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A3.F6 "Figure 6 ‣ Classification datasets. ‣ C.1 Datasets ‣ Appendix C Experimental Setup ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}.
The decision boundaries of networks trained on more epochs are visually
more complex. Hence, the corresponding classification tasks are becoming
more complicated, from the left column to the right column in
Fig. [[7]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A3.F7 "Figure 7 ‣ Classification datasets. ‣ C.1 Datasets ‣ Appendix C Experimental Setup ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}.
In the manuscript, we tested on three out of six datasets, obtained by
snapshots at 10, 80, and 490 epochs, respectively. Given a target
dataset of $n$ classes and $d$ features, [Gaussian pretext
datasets]{#A3.SS1.SSS0.Px1.p1.4.7 .ltx_text .ltx_font_italic} are
generated as follows: using [scikit-learn[^2^[[^2^[[2]{#footnote2.1.1.1
.ltx_text .ltx_font_serif}]{.ltx_tag
.ltx_tag_note}[https://scikit-learn.org/stable/modules/generated/sklearn.datasets.make_classification.html]{#footnote2.5
.ltx_text
.ltx_font_serif}]{.ltx_note_content}]{.ltx_note_outer}]{#footnote2
.ltx_note .ltx_role_footnote}]{#A3.SS1.SSS0.Px1.p1.4.8 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_typewriter}, we randomly generate datasets of $n$ clusters,
where each cluster has 100 normally distributed samples in the
$d$-dimensional space.
:::

<figure id="A3.F7" class="ltx_figure">
<img src="./2206.06565_files/x4.png" id="A3.F7.g1"
class="ltx_graphics ltx_centering ltx_img_landscape" width="830"
height="124" alt="Refer to caption" />
<figcaption><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_figure"><span id="A3.F7.4.1.1"
class="ltx_text" style="font-size:90%;">Figure 7</span>: </span><span
id="A3.F7.5.2" class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold"
style="font-size:90%;">Neural-net-based datasets.<span id="A3.F7.5.2.1"
class="ltx_text ltx_font_medium"> Given <span id="A3.F7.5.2.1.1"
class="ltx_text ltx_font_typewriter">Rolls</span> dataset in Fig. <a
href="https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A3.F6"
class="ltx_ref"
title="Figure 6 ‣ Classification datasets. ‣ C.1 Datasets ‣ Appendix C Experimental Setup ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"><span
class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">6</span></a>, we train a 2-layer neural
network for 10, 40, 80, 210, 320, 490 epochs, and get six decision
boundaries at each column. We define six neural-net-based datasets from
here: each decision boundary is used as a labeling function of each
neural-net-based dataset. In the main manuscript, we used three out of
six datasets, obtained by snapshots at 10, 80, and 490 epochs.
</span></span></figcaption>
</figure>

::: {#A3.SS1.SSS0.Px1.p2 .ltx_para}
([Image datasets]{#A3.SS1.SSS0.Px1.p2.3.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_bold}) For
MNIST-based datasets, as the context length of LMs is limited, we
(center) scale and crop the size of images from $28 \times 28$ into
$18 \times 18$ and use the integral format of pixel values
$\lbrack 0,255\rbrack$. We convert each image into a sequence of pixels
in the order of left to right and top to bottom. Each pixel sequence is
then converted into the sentence input as of our language-interfaced
framework.
:::
:::

::: {#A3.SS1.SSS0.Px2 .section .ltx_paragraph}
##### Regression datasets. {#regression-datasets. .ltx_title .ltx_title_paragraph}

::: {#A3.SS1.SSS0.Px2.p1 .ltx_para}
We test [LIFT]{#A3.SS1.SSS0.Px2.p1.12.1 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps}/GPT on regression problems for both synthetic/real
datasets.\
([Synthetic datasets]{#A3.SS1.SSS0.Px2.p1.12.2 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_bold}) To assess the regression performance of
[LIFT]{#A3.SS1.SSS0.Px2.p1.12.3 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} in
different datasets, we generate synthetic datasets based on six
different functions types, including smooth functions, non-smooth
functions, and non-continuous functions:

1.  [[1.]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_item}]{#A3.I1.i1}

    ::: {#A3.I1.i1.p1 .ltx_para}
    Linear functions
    $y = {f\operatorname{}{({\mathbf{x}})}} = {{{\mathbf{x}}^{\top}\operatorname{}\mathbf{1}}/p}$
    :::
2.  [[2.]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_item}]{#A3.I1.i2}

    ::: {#A3.I1.i2.p1 .ltx_para}
    Quadratic functions
    $y = {f\operatorname{}{({\mathbf{x}})}} = {{{\mathbf{x}}^{T}\operatorname{}{\mathbf{I}}\operatorname{}{\mathbf{x}}}/p}$,
    where $\mathbf{I}$ is the identity matrix
    :::
3.  [[3.]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_item}]{#A3.I1.i3}

    ::: {#A3.I1.i3.p1 .ltx_para}
    Continuous exponential function
    $y = {f\operatorname{}{({\mathbf{x}})}} = {\sum_{i = 1}^{p}{e^{0.2\operatorname{}x_{i}}/p}}$
    :::
4.  [[4.]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_item}]{#A3.I1.i4}

    ::: {#A3.I1.i4.p1 .ltx_para}
    Cosine functions
    $y = {f\operatorname{}{({\mathbf{x}})}} = {\sum_{i = 1}^{p}{{\cos{({0.5\operatorname{}\pi\operatorname{}x_{i}})}}/p}}$
    :::
5.  [[5.]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_item}]{#A3.I1.i5}

    ::: {#A3.I1.i5.p1 .ltx_para}
    (Non-smooth) $\ell\operatorname{}1$-norm function
    $y = {f\operatorname{}{({\mathbf{x}})}} = {\left. \parallel{\mathbf{x}}\parallel \right._{1}/p}$
    :::
6.  [[6.]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_item}]{#A3.I1.i6}

    ::: {#A3.I1.i6.p1 .ltx_para}
    (Non-continuous) Piecewise linear function

      -- --------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- -- ----------------------------------------------------
         $${{f\operatorname{}{(\mathbf{x})}} = {\frac{1}{p}\operatorname{}{\sum\limits_{i = 1}^{p}{\overset{\sim}{f}\operatorname{}{(x_{i})}}}}:=\begin{Bmatrix}      [(1)]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_equation .ltx_align_right}
         {x_{i} - 1} & {{{- 10} \leq x_{i} < {- 3}},} \\                                                                                                              
         0 & {{{- 3} \leq x_{i} < 3},} \\                                                                                                                             
         {x_{i} + 1} & {{3 \leq x_{i} \leq 10}.}                                                                                                                      
         \end{Bmatrix}}.$$                                                                                                                                            
      -- --------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- -- ----------------------------------------------------
    :::

We let $\text{x}_{i} \sim {{Unif}{({- 10},10)}}$ for each coordinate
$i$, and the noise level $\sigma = 0.1$ by default. We normalize all the
functions above for fair comparison among different functions so that
$y \in {\lbrack{- 9},9\rbrack}$ when
${\mathbf{x}} \in {\lbrack{- 10},10\rbrack}^{p}$. In particular, to
assess whether [LIFT]{#A3.SS1.SSS0.Px2.p1.11.1 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps} is better at dealing with positive numbers or
integers, we generate additional datasets by further manipulating the
$(\mathbf{x},\text{y})$ distribution of linear and piecewise functions.
For datasets with real numbers, we generate the 1D dataset
$\text{x} \sim {{Unif}{({- 150},{- 150})}}$. For datasets with only
positive numbers, we generate the dataset
$\text{x}_{i} \sim {{Unif}{(0,300)}}$. To generate the datasets with all
integer prompts, we round down all the features to integers. For
visualization, in addition to the training, validation, and test
datasets, we generated grid datasets. Unless otherwise stated, we
generate uniformly spaced 200 samples for 1D visualizations and 2,500
samples for 2D visualizations, with each coordinate
$\text{x}_{i} \in {\lbrack{- 10},10\rbrack}$ for $i = {1,\ldots,p}$. To
visualize the extrapolation performance, we let the
$\text{x}_{i} \in {\lbrack{- 15},15\rbrack}$.
:::

<figure id="A3.T9" class="ltx_table">
<div id="A3.T9.5"
class="ltx_inline-block ltx_align_center ltx_transformed_outer"
style="width:433.6pt;height:579.7pt;vertical-align:-0.0pt;">
<span class="ltx_transformed_inner"
style="transform:translate(14.8pt,-19.8pt) scale(1.0734785111041,1.0734785111041) ;">
</span>
</div>
<figcaption><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_table"><span id="A3.T9.3.1.1"
class="ltx_text" style="font-size:90%;">Table 9</span>: </span><span
id="A3.T9.4.2" class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold"
style="font-size:90%;">Classification datasets.<span id="A3.T9.4.2.1"
class="ltx_text ltx_font_medium"> We have three non-language types of
data: synthetic data, real tabular data, and vision data. We use five
synthetic datasets. For the real tabular data, we select datasets from
OpenML with a wide range of number of features, types of features,
number of classes, and number of training samples. We use MNIST, Fashion
MNIST, and their permuted variants for the vision
datasets.</span></span></figcaption>
</figure>

::: {#A3.SS1.SSS0.Px2.p2 .ltx_para}
([Real datasets]{#A3.SS1.SSS0.Px2.p2.1.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_bold}) We
consider four real datasets: Medical Insurance
dataset \[[41](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib41){.ltx_ref}\]
with 1,338 samples and 6 features, Combined Cycle Power Plant (CCPP)
dataset \[[42](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib42){.ltx_ref}\]
with 9,568 samples and 4 features,
Servo \[[43](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib43){.ltx_ref}\]
dataset with 167 samples and 4 features, and
Student \[[44](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib44){.ltx_ref}\]
dataset with 649 samples and 33 features. The Medical Insurance and
Student datasets contain feature names that can be interpreted using
common knowledge (see feature lists in Table [[20]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A4.T20 "Table 20 ‣ Regression. ‣ D.2.1 Does LIFT Benefit from Incorporating Feature Names? ‣ D.2 Results for LIFT-Specific Learning Properties (Section 4) ‣ Appendix D Detailed and Extended Results of Experiments in the Main Paper ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}),
while CCPP and Servo do not.
:::
:::
:::

::: {#A3.SS2 .section .ltx_subsection}
### [C.2 ]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_subsection}[LIFT]{#A3.SS2.1.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} and Baseline Implementation {#c.2-lift-and-baseline-implementation .ltx_title .ltx_title_subsection}

::: {#A3.SS2.p1 .ltx_para}
This section provides details of our models and implementation. We
describe our pretrained language models and the baseline implementations
([[C.2.1]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A3.SS2.SSS1 "C.2.1 Pretrained Language Models and Baselines ‣ C.2 LIFT and Baseline Implementation ‣ Appendix C Experimental Setup ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}),
the computing resources used for running experiments ([[C.2.2]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A3.SS2.SSS2 "C.2.2 Computing Resources ‣ C.2 LIFT and Baseline Implementation ‣ Appendix C Experimental Setup ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}),
and how to fine-tune and select the hyperparameters ([[C.2.3]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A3.SS2.SSS3 "C.2.3 Hyperparameter Selection ‣ C.2 LIFT and Baseline Implementation ‣ Appendix C Experimental Setup ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}).
:::

::: {#A3.SS2.SSS1 .section .ltx_subsubsection}
#### [C.2.1 ]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_subsubsection}Pretrained Language Models and Baselines {#c.2.1-pretrained-language-models-and-baselines .ltx_title .ltx_title_subsubsection}

::: {#A3.SS2.SSS1.Px1 .section .ltx_paragraph}
##### Pretrained language models. {#pretrained-language-models. .ltx_title .ltx_title_paragraph}

::: {#A3.SS2.SSS1.Px1.p1 .ltx_para}
Our main results are with two pretrained language models:
GPT-J \[[31](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib31){.ltx_ref}\]
and
GPT-3 \[[19](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib19){.ltx_ref}\].
We mainly focus on GPT-J for the reproducibility purpose and provide
additional results on GPT-3 as reference. For GPT-J, we use a quantized
version[^3^[[^3^[3]{.ltx_tag
.ltx_tag_note}https://huggingface.co/hivemind/gpt-j-6B-8bit]{.ltx_note_content}]{.ltx_note_outer}]{#footnote3
.ltx_note .ltx_role_footnote} of 6 billion parameters with 8-bit
weights. For GPT-3, we use the GPT-3 OpenAI API[^4^[[^4^[4]{.ltx_tag
.ltx_tag_note}https://openai.com/api/]{.ltx_note_content}]{.ltx_note_outer}]{#footnote4
.ltx_note .ltx_role_footnote} and employ the
[Ada]{#A3.SS2.SSS1.Px1.p1.2.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_typewriter} version by
default. In Sec. [[E.2]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A5.SS2 "E.2 Does LIFT Benefit from Larger LMs? ‣ Appendix E Additional Experiments and Findings (NOT Discussed in the Main Paper) ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref},
we compare two previously mentioned models with three bigger versions of
GPT-3 ([Baggage, Curie, Davinci]{#A3.SS2.SSS1.Px1.p1.2.2 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_typewriter}). The largest one is
[Davinci]{#A3.SS2.SSS1.Px1.p1.2.3 .ltx_text .ltx_font_typewriter}-GPT-3
containing approximately $175$ billions of parameters. Since GPTs can
output any language token, the output might not be appropriate for the
desired task. For example, GPT might output non-numerical words for
regression task approximating a function
$f:{{\mathbb{R}}^{n}\rightarrow{\mathbb{R}}}$. In such a case, we
categorize this as [invalid output]{#A3.SS2.SSS1.Px1.p1.2.4 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_italic}.
:::
:::

::: {#A3.SS2.SSS1.Px2 .section .ltx_paragraph}
##### Baselines. {#baselines. .ltx_title .ltx_title_paragraph}

::: {#A3.SS2.SSS1.Px2.p1 .ltx_para}
For XGBoost, we use the open-source [XGBoost]{#A3.SS2.SSS1.Px2.p1.1.1
.ltx_text .ltx_font_typewriter} module[^5^[[^5^[5]{.ltx_tag
.ltx_tag_note}https://xgboost.readthedocs.io/]{.ltx_note_content}]{.ltx_note_outer}]{#footnote5
.ltx_note .ltx_role_footnote}. For other baselines, we implement them
using [scikit-learn]{#A3.SS2.SSS1.Px2.p1.1.2 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_typewriter} module[^6^[[^6^[6]{.ltx_tag
.ltx_tag_note}https://scikit-learn.org/]{.ltx_note_content}]{.ltx_note_outer}]{#footnote6
.ltx_note .ltx_role_footnote}.
:::
:::
:::

::: {#A3.SS2.SSS2 .section .ltx_subsubsection}
#### [C.2.2 ]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_subsubsection}Computing Resources {#c.2.2-computing-resources .ltx_title .ltx_title_subsubsection}

::: {#A3.SS2.SSS2.p1 .ltx_para}
For experiments on GPT-J, we use GPU computing from two 24GB-RTX3090
GPUs and AWS EC2 instances[^7^[[^7^[7]{.ltx_tag
.ltx_tag_note}https://aws.amazon.com/ec2/]{.ltx_note_content}]{.ltx_note_outer}]{#footnote7
.ltx_note .ltx_role_footnote} ([p3.8xlarge]{#A3.SS2.SSS2.p1.1.1
.ltx_text .ltx_font_typewriter}, [p3.2xlarge]{#A3.SS2.SSS2.p1.1.2
.ltx_text .ltx_font_typewriter}). For other models, we run experiments
on CPU instances.
:::
:::

::: {#A3.SS2.SSS3 .section .ltx_subsubsection}
#### [C.2.3 ]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_subsubsection}Hyperparameter Selection {#c.2.3-hyperparameter-selection .ltx_title .ltx_title_subsubsection}

::: {#A3.SS2.SSS3.p1 .ltx_para}
In fine-tuning GPT-J, we use Adam-8bit optimizer
implementation[^8^[[^8^[8]{.ltx_tag
.ltx_tag_note}https://huggingface.co/hivemind/gpt-j-6B-8bit]{.ltx_note_content}]{.ltx_note_outer}]{#footnote8
.ltx_note .ltx_role_footnote} with weight decay of $0.01$ and $6$
warm-up steps. The learning rate is chosen from
[1e-4]{#A3.SS2.SSS3.p1.3.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_typewriter} and
[2e-4]{#A3.SS2.SSS3.p1.3.2 .ltx_text .ltx_font_typewriter} for the
synthetic/OpenML datasets, and [1e-5]{#A3.SS2.SSS3.p1.3.3 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_typewriter} for the vision datasets. We use a linear learning
scheduler for the optimizer. For classification, the batch size depends
on the number of features of the datasets. We set the batch size to be
128, 32, 16, and 2 for datasets with the number of features being no
greater than 2, between 2 and 6, between 6 and 20, and greater than 20,
respectively. For regression, we set batch size as $4$ by default and
reduce it to 1 to avoid the memory issue when the number of features
increases. For GPT-3, we use the API provided by OpenAI to perform
black-box GPT-3 fine-tuning with the default setting. Our implementation
is with PyTorch framework[^9^[[^9^[9]{.ltx_tag
.ltx_tag_note}https://pytorch.org/]{.ltx_note_content}]{.ltx_note_outer}]{#footnote9
.ltx_note .ltx_role_footnote}. We perform hyperparameter selection based
on validation results for all methods for a fair comparison. The
hyperparameter tuning scheme for all methods is detailed as follows:
:::

::: {#A3.SS2.SSS3.Px1 .section .ltx_paragraph}
##### Classification methods. {#classification-methods. .ltx_title .ltx_title_paragraph}

::: {#A3.SS2.SSS3.Px1.p1 .ltx_para}
-   [[•]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_item}]{#A3.I2.i1}

    ::: {#A3.I2.i1.p1 .ltx_para}
    [LIFT]{#A3.I2.i1.p1.1.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps}/GPT-J: number
    of epochs $\in {\{ 5,10,15\}}$
    :::
-   [[•]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_item}]{#A3.I2.i2}

    ::: {#A3.I2.i2.p1 .ltx_para}
    [LIFT]{#A3.I2.i2.p1.1.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps}/GPT-3:
    learning rate multiplier $\in {\{ 0.05,0.1,0.2\}}$
    :::
-   [[•]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_item}]{#A3.I2.i3}

    ::: {#A3.I2.i3.p1 .ltx_para}
    Random Forest ([RF]{#A3.I2.i3.p1.2.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_italic}):
    maximum depth $\in {\{ 3,5,10\}}$, minimum number of samples
    required to split an internal node $\in {\{ 2,5,10\}}$
    :::
-   [[•]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_item}]{#A3.I2.i4}

    ::: {#A3.I2.i4.p1 .ltx_para}
    Decision Tree ([DT]{#A3.I2.i4.p1.2.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_italic}):
    maximum depth of the tree $\in {\{ 3,5,20\}}$ and criterion
    $\in {\{\text{Gini impurity, Shannon information gain}\}}$
    :::
-   [[•]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_item}]{#A3.I2.i5}

    ::: {#A3.I2.i5.p1 .ltx_para}
    Support Vector Machine ([SVM]{#A3.I2.i5.p1.2.1 .ltx_text
    .ltx_font_italic}): kernel
    $\in {\{\text{linear kernel, radial basis function}\}}$,
    regularization parameter $\in {\{ 1,10,100\}}$
    :::
-   [[•]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_item}]{#A3.I2.i6}

    ::: {#A3.I2.i6.p1 .ltx_para}
    Multilayer Perceptron ([MLP]{#A3.I2.i6.p1.1.1 .ltx_text
    .ltx_font_italic}): initial learning rate
    $\in {\{ 0.001,0.01,0.1\}}$
    :::
-   [[•]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_item}]{#A3.I2.i7}

    ::: {#A3.I2.i7.p1 .ltx_para}
    Logistic regression ([LogReg]{#A3.I2.i7.p1.1.1 .ltx_text
    .ltx_font_italic}): inverse of regularization strength
    $\in {\{ 1,10,100\}}$
    :::
-   [[•]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_item}]{#A3.I2.i8}

    ::: {#A3.I2.i8.p1 .ltx_para}
    K-Nearest Neighbor ([KNN]{#A3.I2.i8.p1.2.1 .ltx_text
    .ltx_font_italic}): number of neighbors to use $\in {\{ 1,3,5\}}$,
    power parameter for the Minkowski metric $\in {\{ 1,2\}}$
    :::
-   [[•]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_item}]{#A3.I2.i9}

    ::: {#A3.I2.i9.p1 .ltx_para}
    XGBoost ([XG]{#A3.I2.i9.p1.1.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_italic}): maximum
    depth $\in {\{ 3,5,10\}}$
    :::
:::
:::

::: {#A3.SS2.SSS3.Px2 .section .ltx_paragraph}
##### Regression methods {#regression-methods .ltx_title .ltx_title_paragraph}

::: {#A3.SS2.SSS3.Px2.p1 .ltx_para}
-   [[•]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_item}]{#A3.I3.i1}

    ::: {#A3.I3.i1.p1 .ltx_para}
    [LIFT]{#A3.I3.i1.p1.1.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps}/GPT-J: number
    of epochs $\in {\{ 2,6,10\}}$
    :::
-   [[•]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_item}]{#A3.I3.i2}

    ::: {#A3.I3.i2.p1 .ltx_para}
    [LIFT]{#A3.I3.i2.p1.1.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps}/GPT-3:
    learning rate multiplier $\in {\{ 0.05,0.1,0.2\}}$
    :::
-   [[•]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_item}]{#A3.I3.i3}

    ::: {#A3.I3.i3.p1 .ltx_para}
    Polynomial Regression ([PR]{#A3.I3.i3.p1.1.1 .ltx_text
    .ltx_font_italic}): no hyperparameter selection but fixed the degree
    at $3$ since higher-order polynomial regression introduces
    out-of-memory error, especially for high-dimensional datasets
    :::
-   [[•]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_item}]{#A3.I3.i4}

    ::: {#A3.I3.i4.p1 .ltx_para}
    K-Nearest Neighbor ([KNN]{#A3.I3.i4.p1.1.1 .ltx_text
    .ltx_font_italic}): number of neighbors $\in {\{ 2,5,8\}}$
    :::
-   [[•]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_item}]{#A3.I3.i5}

    ::: {#A3.I3.i5.p1 .ltx_para}
    Kernel Regression ([KR]{#A3.I3.i5.p1.1.1 .ltx_text
    .ltx_font_italic}): Gamma parameter of Radial Basis Kernel
    $\in {\{ 0.01,0.1,1\}}$
    :::
-   [[•]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_item}]{#A3.I3.i6}

    ::: {#A3.I3.i6.p1 .ltx_para}
    Multilayer Perceptron ([MLP]{#A3.I3.i6.p1.1.1 .ltx_text
    .ltx_font_italic}): initial learning rate
    $\in {\{ 0.0001,0.001,0.01\}}$
    :::
-   [[•]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_item}]{#A3.I3.i7}

    ::: {#A3.I3.i7.p1 .ltx_para}
    Gradient Boosting Tree ([GBT]{#A3.I3.i7.p1.1.1 .ltx_text
    .ltx_font_italic}): learning rate $\in {\{ 0.001,0.01,0.1\}}$
    :::
-   [[•]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_item}]{#A3.I3.i8}

    ::: {#A3.I3.i8.p1 .ltx_para}
    Random Forest ([RF]{#A3.I3.i8.p1.1.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_italic}):
    maximum depth $\in {\{ 4,6\}}$
    :::
-   [[•]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_item}]{#A3.I3.i9}

    ::: {#A3.I3.i9.p1 .ltx_para}
    Gaussian Process ([GP]{#A3.I3.i9.p1.1.1 .ltx_text
    .ltx_font_italic}): the number of optimizer restarts used to find
    parameters of the kernel that maximize the log marginal likelihood
    $\in {\{ 5,10\}}$.
    :::

Note that we perform model selection based on validation RAE, instead of
validation loss.
:::
:::
:::
:::
:::

::: {#A4 .section .ltx_appendix}
## [Appendix D ]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_appendix}Detailed and Extended Results of Experiments in the Main Paper {#appendix-d-detailed-and-extended-results-of-experiments-in-the-main-paper .ltx_title .ltx_title_appendix}

::: {#A4.p1 .ltx_para}
In this section, we provide the extended version of experimental results
presented in the main paper, including the primary findings of
[LIFT]{#A4.p1.1.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} ([[D.1]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A4.SS1 "D.1 Results for Basic Findings of LIFT (Section 3) ‣ Appendix D Detailed and Extended Results of Experiments in the Main Paper ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}),
the specific learning properties of [LIFT]{#A4.p1.1.2 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps} ([[D.2]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A4.SS2 "D.2 Results for LIFT-Specific Learning Properties (Section 4) ‣ Appendix D Detailed and Extended Results of Experiments in the Main Paper ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}),
and improving techniques for [LIFT]{#A4.p1.1.3 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps} ([[D.3]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A4.SS3 "D.3 Results for Improving Techniques of LIFT (Section 5) ‣ Appendix D Detailed and Extended Results of Experiments in the Main Paper ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}).
:::

::: {#A4.SS1 .section .ltx_subsection}
### [D.1 ]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_subsection}Results for Basic Findings of [LIFT]{#A4.SS1.1.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} (Section [[3]{.ltx_text .ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#S3 "3 Basic Findings of LIFT ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}) {#d.1-results-for-basic-findings-of-lift-section-3 .ltx_title .ltx_title_subsection}

::: {#A4.SS1.SSS1 .section .ltx_subsubsection}
#### [D.1.1 ]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_subsubsection}How Well Does [LIFT]{#A4.SS1.SSS1.1.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} Perform on Standard ML Tasks? {#d.1.1-how-well-does-lift-perform-on-standard-ml-tasks .ltx_title .ltx_title_subsubsection}
