<figcaption><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_table"><span id="A5.T23.90.2.1"
class="ltx_text" style="font-size:90%;">Table 23</span>: </span><span
id="A5.T23.2.1" class="ltx_text" style="font-size:90%;"> <span
id="A5.T23.2.1.1" class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold">The effects of larger
LMs under different classification settings</span>. Recall that our
previous results on GPT-3 are based on the smallest model <span
id="A5.T23.2.1.2" class="ltx_text ltx_font_typewriter">Ada</span>. Here
we use larger GPT-3 versions (<span id="A5.T23.2.1.3"
class="ltx_text ltx_font_typewriter">Babbage, Curie, Davinci</span>) as
the pretrained LMs in our framework and evaluate the classification
accuracy (<span class="math inline">↑</span>) of them in three settings:
classification <span id="A5.T23.2.1.4"
class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic">without</span> feature name,
classification <span id="A5.T23.2.1.5"
class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic">with</span> feature name and in-context
classification. For the setting of classification <span
id="A5.T23.2.1.6" class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic">with</span> feature
names, we incorporate names of features (columns) into the input prompts
(see more details in Sec. <a
href="https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#S4.SS1"
class="ltx_ref"
title="4.1 Does LIFT Benefit from Incorporating Feature Names? ‣ 4 Evaluation of LIFT-Specific Learning Properties ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"><span
class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">4.1</span></a>). For in-context learning,
the OpenML dataset ID and number of prompts are written together at each
column, <span id="A5.T23.2.1.7"
class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic">e.g.</span>, <span id="A5.T23.2.1.8"
class="ltx_text ltx_font_typewriter">TAE</span> (48)/50 means that we
run experiments on the OpenML dataset <span id="A5.T23.2.1.9"
class="ltx_text ltx_font_typewriter">TAE</span> having ID 48, by using
50 input prompts. For the first two settings when <span
id="A5.T23.2.1.10" class="ltx_text ltx_font_smallcaps">LIFT</span> is
applied, larger <span id="A5.T23.2.1.11"
class="ltx_text ltx_font_smallcaps">LIFT</span>/GPT-3 models (<span
id="A5.T23.2.1.12" class="ltx_text ltx_font_typewriter">Babbage, Curie,
Davinci</span>) perform better than the smaller models <span
id="A5.T23.2.1.13"
class="ltx_text ltx_font_smallcaps">LIFT</span>/GPT-3-<span
id="A5.T23.2.1.14" class="ltx_text ltx_font_typewriter">Ada</span> and
<span id="A5.T23.2.1.15"
class="ltx_text ltx_font_smallcaps">LIFT</span>/GPT-J, but the
performance gains are not always consistent and significant with model
sizes. For the in-context classification (<span id="A5.T23.2.1.16"
class="ltx_text ltx_font_smallcaps">LIFT</span> is not used), we observe
more consistent improvement by using larger models.</span></figcaption>
</figure>

<figure id="A5.T24" class="ltx_table">
<figcaption><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_table"><span id="A5.T24.38.2.1"
class="ltx_text" style="font-size:90%;">Table 24</span>: </span><span
id="A5.T24.2.1" class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold"
style="font-size:90%;">Comparison of <span id="A5.T24.2.1.2"
class="ltx_text ltx_font_smallcaps">LIFT</span> on different LMs across
regression tasks.<span id="A5.T24.2.1.1"
class="ltx_text ltx_font_medium"> The regression performance is measured
by RAE (<span class="math inline">↓</span>). In general, <span
id="A5.T24.2.1.1.1"
class="ltx_text ltx_font_smallcaps">LIFT</span>/GPT-3 with <span
id="A5.T24.2.1.1.2" class="ltx_text ltx_font_typewriter">Davinci</span>
model performs the best, but the gaps to other models are not always
significant.</span></span></figcaption>
</figure>

::: {#A5.SS2.p1 .ltx_para}
In this experiment, we apply [LIFT]{#A5.SS2.p1.1.1 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps} to different pretrained LMs to verify whether
[LIFT]{#A5.SS2.p1.1.2 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} benefits more from
larger LMs. Together with previously used GPT-J and GPT-3 (the version
named [Ada]{#A5.SS2.p1.1.3 .ltx_text .ltx_font_typewriter}), we consider
three bigger versions of GPT-3, namely [Baggage]{#A5.SS2.p1.1.4
.ltx_text .ltx_font_typewriter}, [Curie]{#A5.SS2.p1.1.5 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_typewriter}, and [Davinci]{#A5.SS2.p1.1.6 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_typewriter} (in the ascending order of the number of
parameters). We compare all models on several classification tasks in
Table [[23]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A5.T23 "Table 23 ‣ E.2 Does LIFT Benefit from Larger LMs? ‣ Appendix E Additional Experiments and Findings (NOT Discussed in the Main Paper) ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}
and regression tasks in Table [[24]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A5.T24 "Table 24 ‣ E.2 Does LIFT Benefit from Larger LMs? ‣ Appendix E Additional Experiments and Findings (NOT Discussed in the Main Paper) ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}.
Overall, we find that the performance gain of using larger LMs is not
consistently significant for [LIFT]{#A5.SS2.p1.1.7 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps}. Although larger LMs outperform smaller LMs in many
cases, the improvements are relatively small.
:::

::: {#A5.SS2.SSS0.Px1 .section .ltx_paragraph}
##### Verifying the capability of large LMs, when [LIFT]{#A5.SS2.SSS0.Px1.1.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} is not used. {#verifying-the-capability-of-large-lms-when-lift-is-not-used. .ltx_title .ltx_title_paragraph}

::: {#A5.SS2.SSS0.Px1.p1 .ltx_para}
We first verify if larger LMs are more helpful for the evaluated
downstream tasks. We evaluate LMs in the in-context classification when
no fine-tuning ([LIFT]{#A5.SS2.SSS0.Px1.p1.1.1 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps}) is involved. Table [[23]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A5.T23 "Table 23 ‣ E.2 Does LIFT Benefit from Larger LMs? ‣ Appendix E Additional Experiments and Findings (NOT Discussed in the Main Paper) ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}
shows consistent improvements in classification performance when the
size of LMs increases across all the tasks. Thus, larger LMs, with
larger embedded knowledge, are more useful for these downstream tasks.
:::
:::

::: {#A5.SS2.SSS0.Px2 .section .ltx_paragraph}
##### When [LIFT]{#A5.SS2.SSS0.Px2.1.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} is used. {#when-lift-is-used. .ltx_title .ltx_title_paragraph}

::: {#A5.SS2.SSS0.Px2.p1 .ltx_para}
Both Table [[23]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A5.T23 "Table 23 ‣ E.2 Does LIFT Benefit from Larger LMs? ‣ Appendix E Additional Experiments and Findings (NOT Discussed in the Main Paper) ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}
and Table [[24]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A5.T24 "Table 24 ‣ E.2 Does LIFT Benefit from Larger LMs? ‣ Appendix E Additional Experiments and Findings (NOT Discussed in the Main Paper) ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}
show that using larger LMs may positively affect
[LIFT]{#A5.SS2.SSS0.Px2.p1.1.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} in several
tasks and settings compared to the smaller LMs. However, the performance
gains from replacing the smaller LMs with larger LMs are not consistent
across the settings. For instance, in the classification settings
without feature names, [Davinci]{#A5.SS2.SSS0.Px2.p1.1.2 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_typewriter} performs better than GPT-J on four datasets and
worse than on one dataset. For the setting with feature names, GPT-J
performs better than [Davinci]{#A5.SS2.SSS0.Px2.p1.1.3 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_typewriter} on two out of three tasks. Furthermore, the
performance gains of large LMs over the smaller models are not
relatively significant. We note that [LIFT]{#A5.SS2.SSS0.Px2.p1.1.4
.ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} always outperforms the in-context
learning using the same pretrained LMs in most cases. The regression
results shown in Table [[24]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A5.T24 "Table 24 ‣ E.2 Does LIFT Benefit from Larger LMs? ‣ Appendix E Additional Experiments and Findings (NOT Discussed in the Main Paper) ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}
further confirm that the improvement from utilizing larger LMs is
relatively small.
:::
:::
:::

::: {#A5.SS3 .section .ltx_subsection}
### [E.3 ]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_subsection}Quantitative Classification Evaluations on Neural-Net-Based Synthetic Datasets {#e.3-quantitative-classification-evaluations-on-neural-net-based-synthetic-datasets .ltx_title .ltx_title_subsection}

<figure id="A5.F20" class="ltx_figure">
<img src="./2206.06565_files/x22.png" id="A5.F20.g1"
class="ltx_graphics ltx_centering ltx_img_landscape" width="706"
height="227" alt="Refer to caption" />
<figcaption><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_figure"><span id="A5.F20.9.4.1"
class="ltx_text" style="font-size:90%;">Figure 20</span>: </span><span
id="A5.F20.6.3" class="ltx_text" style="font-size:90%;"> <span
id="A5.F20.4.1.1" class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold">How accuracy (<span
class="math inline">↑</span>) changes as the target classification
problem becomes more complex, i.e., the ground-truth decision boundary
becomes more complex. </span> The x-axis shows the number of epochs we
used to train the neural network on the <span id="A5.F20.6.3.2"
class="ltx_text ltx_font_typewriter">Rolls</span> dataset. Note that the
network becomes more complex as the number of epochs increases. Thus the
classification problem also gets challenging. We measure the
performances on three cases: (left) clean data, (middle) label-corrupted
data with corruption probability <span class="math inline">5%</span> and
(right) <span class="math inline">20%</span>.</span></figcaption>
</figure>

::: {#A5.SS3.p1 .ltx_para}
In Sec. [[3.4]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#S3.SS4 "3.4 Can We Understand the Inductive Biases of Language Models via LIFT? ‣ 3 Basic Findings of LIFT ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}
and Appendix [[D.1.3]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A4.SS1.SSS3 "D.1.3 Can We Understand the Inductive Biases of Language Models via LIFT? ‣ D.1 Results for Basic Findings of LIFT (Section 3) ‣ Appendix D Detailed and Extended Results of Experiments in the Main Paper ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref},
we assess how well [LIFT]{#A5.SS3.p1.3.1 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps}/GPTs adapt to different shapes of decision
boundaries on neural-net-based synthetic datasets. We now provide the
test accuracies for all models. [For binary-class
datasets,]{#A5.SS3.p1.3.2 .ltx_text .ltx_font_italic}
Fig. [[20]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A5.F20 "Figure 20 ‣ E.3 Quantitative Classification Evaluations on Neural-Net-Based Synthetic Datasets ‣ Appendix E Additional Experiments and Findings (NOT Discussed in the Main Paper) ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}
shows the accuracies on three binary-class datasets when the difficulty
of classification tasks varies by using different network checkpoints at
different epochs. As the difficulty increases or the level of corruption
increases, all methods tend to decrease classification accuracy. We
provide three settings of training data: clean data, 5% label
corruption, and 20% label corruption. We observe that
[LIFT]{#A5.SS3.p1.3.3 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps}/GPT-3 outperforms
logistic regression and decision tree, especially when the label
corruption is up to 20%. However, [LIFT]{#A5.SS3.p1.3.4 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps}/GPT-J is performing worse than other baselines in
the data corruption scenarios. [For 3-class and 5-class
datasets,]{#A5.SS3.p1.3.5 .ltx_text .ltx_font_italic} both
[LIFT]{#A5.SS3.p1.3.6 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps}/GPT-J and
[LIFT]{#A5.SS3.p1.3.7 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps}/GPT-3 achieve
approximately $90\%$, while the best baselines (MLP and XGBoost) obtains
approximately $92\%$ and $91\%$ for the 3-class and 5-class data,
respectively.
:::
:::

::: {#A5.SS4 .section .ltx_subsection}
### [E.4 ]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_subsection}Can [LIFT]{#A5.SS4.1.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} Perform Ridge Regression via Data Augmentation? {#e.4-can-lift-perform-ridge-regression-via-data-augmentation .ltx_title .ltx_title_subsection}

<figure id="A5.T25" class="ltx_table">
<figcaption><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_table"><span id="A5.T25.69.2.1"
class="ltx_text" style="font-size:90%;">Table 25</span>: </span><span
id="A5.T25.2.1" class="ltx_text" style="font-size:90%;"> <span
id="A5.T25.2.1.1" class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold">Performance of <span
id="A5.T25.2.1.1.1" class="ltx_text ltx_font_smallcaps">LIFT</span> on
Ridge regression.</span> We measure the RAE (<span
class="math inline">↓</span>) of <span id="A5.T25.2.1.2"
class="ltx_text ltx_font_smallcaps">LIFT</span> corresponding to Linear
Regression (LR) and Ridge Regression. The RAEs indicate that <span
id="A5.T25.2.1.3" class="ltx_text ltx_font_smallcaps">LIFT</span> does
not perform well on the Ridge regression problem. </span></figcaption>
</figure>

::: {#A5.SS4.p1 .ltx_para}
As shown in Fig. [[2]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#S1.F2 "Figure 2 ‣ 1 Introduction ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref},
[LIFT]{#A5.SS4.p1.6.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} can perform linear
regression. We take one step further and study whether
[LIFT]{#A5.SS4.p1.6.2 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} can perform Ridge
regression. Note that this is a non-trivial task as the
[LIFT]{#A5.SS4.p1.6.3 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} framework does not
allow any changes to the loss function. Consider a standard ridge
regression problem solving the optimal $\mathbf{w}$ with $p$ parameters
so that
${\|{{\mathbf{y}} - {{\mathbf{X}}\operatorname{}{\mathbf{w}}}}\|}_{2}^{2} + {\lambda\operatorname{}{\|{\mathbf{w}}\|}_{2}^{2}}$
is minimized. Note that this problem is equivalent to minimizing
${\|{{\lbrack{\mathbf{y}}^{T},0\rbrack}^{T} - {{\lbrack{\mathbf{X}}^{T},{\sqrt{\lambda}\operatorname{}{\mathbf{I}}}\rbrack}^{T}\operatorname{}{\mathbf{w}}}}\|}_{2}^{2}$.
Therefore, if we add $p$ additional training samples
$\sqrt{\lambda}\operatorname{}I$, one can perform ridge regression via
data augmentation. Inspired by this, we study whether one can perform
ridge regression via data augmentation within the framework of the
[LIFT]{#A5.SS4.p1.6.4 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} framework. The
results of [LIFT]{#A5.SS4.p1.6.5 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} on Ridge
regression are reported in Table [[25]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A5.T25 "Table 25 ‣ E.4 Can LIFT Perform Ridge Regression via Data Augmentation? ‣ Appendix E Additional Experiments and Findings (NOT Discussed in the Main Paper) ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}.
We observe that [LIFT]{#A5.SS4.p1.6.6 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps}
fails to perform Ridge regression. This is expected, as
[LIFT]{#A5.SS4.p1.6.7 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} is shown to be
robust to outliers (in Sec. [[3.5]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#S3.SS5 "3.5 How Robust is LIFT? ‣ 3 Basic Findings of LIFT ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}
and Appendix [[D.1.4]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A4.SS1.SSS4 "D.1.4 How Robust Is LIFT? ‣ D.1 Results for Basic Findings of LIFT (Section 3) ‣ Appendix D Detailed and Extended Results of Experiments in the Main Paper ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}).
:::
:::

::: {#A5.SS5 .section .ltx_subsection}
### [E.5 ]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_subsection}[LIFT]{#A5.SS5.1.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps}'s Training Curve {#e.5-lifts-training-curve .ltx_title .ltx_title_subsection}

::: {#A5.SS5.p1 .ltx_para}
We report the learning curves of [LIFT]{#A5.SS5.p1.1.1 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps} in terms of LM-loss and accuracies/RAE for several
classification and regression tasks. We observe a decrease in training
loss over the tasks and datasets. We select the best models based on the
validation criteria (accuracy for classification and RAE for regression)
on the validation sets. Fig. [[21]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A5.F21 "Figure 21 ‣ E.5 LIFT’s Training Curve ‣ Appendix E Additional Experiments and Findings (NOT Discussed in the Main Paper) ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}
visualize the accuracy and loss of [LIFT]{#A5.SS5.p1.1.2 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps}/GPT-J in the training and validation process for
classification tasks. For the regression task, Fig. [[22]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A5.F22 "Figure 22 ‣ E.5 LIFT’s Training Curve ‣ Appendix E Additional Experiments and Findings (NOT Discussed in the Main Paper) ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}
shows that the decrease in RAE does not necessarily imply a decrease in
loss. Furthermore, we observe that [LIFT]{#A5.SS5.p1.1.3 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps} only requires a few epochs to achieve good
performance.
:::

<figure id="A5.F21" class="ltx_figure">

::: {#A6 .section .ltx_appendix}
## [Appendix F ]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_appendix}Additional Discussion {#appendix-f-additional-discussion .ltx_title .ltx_title_appendix}

::: {#A6.p1 .ltx_para}
Continuing from Sec. [[7]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#S7 "7 Discussion and Conclusion ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref},
here we elaborate on the difficulty of regression tasks and the broader
impact of [LIFT]{#A6.p1.1.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps}, and discuss
the limitation on classification tasks and other open questions.
:::

::: {#A6.SS1 .section .ltx_subsection}
### [F.1 ]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_subsection}Limitations and Open Questions {#f.1-limitations-and-open-questions .ltx_title .ltx_title_subsection}

::: {#A6.SS1.SSS0.Px1 .section .ltx_paragraph}
##### The difficulty of regression tasks. {#the-difficulty-of-regression-tasks. .ltx_title .ltx_title_paragraph}

::: {#A6.SS1.SSS0.Px1.p1 .ltx_para}
For regression tasks, in addition to poor performance on
high-dimensional functions, some interesting phenomena observed in the
classification tasks are not consistently observed in the regression
tasks. For example, incorporating feature names in the prompts does not
consistently improve [LIFT]{#A6.SS1.SSS0.Px1.p1.14.1 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps} in the regression tasks (see
Sec. [[D.2.1]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A4.SS2.SSS1 "D.2.1 Does LIFT Benefit from Incorporating Feature Names? ‣ D.2 Results for LIFT-Specific Learning Properties (Section 4) ‣ Appendix D Detailed and Extended Results of Experiments in the Main Paper ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}).
As previously discussed in Sec. [[3.1]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#S3.SS1 "3.1 How Well Does LIFT Perform on Standard ML Tasks? ‣ 3 Basic Findings of LIFT ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref},
the difficulty of regression tasks on [LIFT]{#A6.SS1.SSS0.Px1.p1.14.2
.ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} may come from the classification loss
function used in LMs. Due to the adoption of the classification loss
function, two different predictions will lead to the same loss, even if
one of the predictions is closer to the true $y$ value. As a result, we
also observe that a reduction in RAE does not necessarily imply a
reduction in LM loss (see Fig. [[22]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#A5.F22 "Figure 22 ‣ E.5 LIFT’s Training Curve ‣ Appendix E Additional Experiments and Findings (NOT Discussed in the Main Paper) ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}).
Therefore, we use RAE as the criterion for model selection. Moreover,
how [LIFT]{#A6.SS1.SSS0.Px1.p1.14.3 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps}
understands numerical values may also limit the regression performance
of [LIFT]{#A6.SS1.SSS0.Px1.p1.14.4 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps}.
Recent
works \[[170](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib170){.ltx_ref},
[135](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib135){.ltx_ref},
[171](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib171){.ltx_ref},
[172](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib172){.ltx_ref}\]
have illustrated the difficulty and failures of the existing LMs in
understanding the numbers because two numbers with close values can have
very different
tokenizations \[[135](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib135){.ltx_ref}\].
Recent
attempts \[[133](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib133){.ltx_ref},
[173](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib173){.ltx_ref},
[174](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib174){.ltx_ref},
[175](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib175){.ltx_ref},
[176](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib176){.ltx_ref}\]
propose new encoding schemes of numbers to improve the LMs' numerical
capabilities, probably helping [LIFT]{#A6.SS1.SSS0.Px1.p1.14.5 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps} in the regression tasks. A promising method for
improving [LIFT]{#A6.SS1.SSS0.Px1.p1.14.6 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps}
on regression is [level encoding]{#A6.SS1.SSS0.Px1.p1.14.7 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_italic}. The idea of level encoding is to discretize the
continuous values of the output $y$ to better utilize the classification
loss of LMs. Assuming that the range of $y$ is known, we can partition
this range into a finite number of bins and represent all values in the
same bin by a unique canonical representation in a way that the number
of mismatched bits between the representations of two values is
proportional to their absolute difference. For instance, for all
real-value $y \in {\lbrack 0,3\rbrack}$, we can define three bins as
$\{{\lbrack 0,1)},{\lbrack 1,2)},{\lbrack 2,3\rbrack}\}$ with the
canonical representations being $00,01,11$. With these bins, 0.3 and 0.7
are represented as $00$, and $1.5$ and $1.1$ are represented as $01$.
The distance between representations of $0.3$ and $1.1$ is only $1$ bit,
which is proportional to their absolute distance of $0.8$. For the
training of [LIFT]{#A6.SS1.SSS0.Px1.p1.14.8 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps}, we convert all output values in the original
training dataset into the level-encoding canonical representation and
use them as the target values. By using the level encoding technique,
the loss function of LMs can better capture the distance between the
prediction and the true values, thus potentially improving the
generalization of [LIFT]{#A6.SS1.SSS0.Px1.p1.14.9 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps} on regression tasks. We leave this as one of the
interesting directions for our future investigation.
:::
:::

::: {#A6.SS1.SSS0.Px2 .section .ltx_paragraph}
##### The limitation of [LIFT]{#A6.SS1.SSS0.Px2.1.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} on classification tasks. {#the-limitation-of-lift-on-classification-tasks. .ltx_title .ltx_title_paragraph}

::: {#A6.SS1.SSS0.Px2.p1 .ltx_para}
We observe that [LIFT]{#A6.SS1.SSS0.Px2.p1.1.1 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps} does not perform comparably well on classification
tasks when the number of classes is large. For instance,
Table [[2]{.ltx_text
.ltx_ref_tag}](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#S3.T2 "Table 2 ‣ 3.1 How Well Does LIFT Perform on Standard ML Tasks? ‣ 3 Basic Findings of LIFT ‣ LIFT: Language-Interfaced Fine-Tuning for Non-Language Machine Learning Tasks"){.ltx_ref}
shows that the accuracies of [LIFT]{#A6.SS1.SSS0.Px2.p1.1.2 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_smallcaps}/GPT-3 are lower than RBF-SVM and XGboost on the
datasets with 100 classes. Another limitation is that the dimension of
features [LIFT]{#A6.SS1.SSS0.Px2.p1.1.3 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps}
can handle is upper bounded due to the limited context length of LMs.
This limitation may be mitigated by using LMs with a more
memory-efficient variant or implementation of transformer models,
[e.g.]{#A6.SS1.SSS0.Px2.p1.1.4 .ltx_text .ltx_font_italic}, see
\[[177](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib177){.ltx_ref}\].
:::
:::

::: {#A6.SS1.SSS0.Px3 .section .ltx_paragraph}
##### Other open questions. {#other-open-questions. .ltx_title .ltx_title_paragraph}

::: {#A6.SS1.SSS0.Px3.p1 .ltx_para}
In addition to previously discussed questions of improving
[LIFT]{#A6.SS1.SSS0.Px3.p1.1.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} for
regression and classification tasks, our pioneering work on
[LIFT]{#A6.SS1.SSS0.Px3.p1.1.2 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} is also
expected to open up interesting research questions on generalist models.
First, can generalist LMs ([e.g.]{#A6.SS1.SSS0.Px3.p1.1.3 .ltx_text
.ltx_font_italic}, GPTs) play a leading role in developing universal
models that can adapt well to any data? Second, can we apply
[LIFT]{#A6.SS1.SSS0.Px3.p1.1.4 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} to
different generalist models, such as
GATO \[[90](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib90){.ltx_ref}\]?
:::
:::
:::

::: {#A6.SS2 .section .ltx_subsection}
### [F.2 ]{.ltx_tag .ltx_tag_subsection}Broader Impact {#f.2-broader-impact .ltx_title .ltx_title_subsection}

::: {#A6.SS2.p1 .ltx_para}
[LIFT]{#A6.SS2.p1.1.1 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} greatly simplifies
the machine learning pipeline that requires only the reformatting of
training datasets of the target task. This simplicity helps enable
[no-code ML]{#A6.SS2.p1.1.2 .ltx_text .ltx_font_italic} for the masses,
where general users without prior knowledge of the ML frameworks can use
[LIFT]{#A6.SS2.p1.1.3 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} for their target
non-language tasks by properly designing the input/output prompt format.
Therefore, [LIFT]{#A6.SS2.p1.1.4 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} can
apply to a wide range of applications and areas, such as credit loaning,
disease diagnosis, and criminal sentencing. This is closely related to
the line of automated machine learning
research \[[91](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib91){.ltx_ref},
[92](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib92){.ltx_ref}\],
which aims to automate the standard machine learning methods pipeline.
Employing [LIFT]{#A6.SS2.p1.1.5 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} without
careful justification or understanding will lead to undesired outcomes,
such as discrimination. Since most existing language models (LMs) are
pretrained on a large amount of human-annotated data,
[LIFT]{#A6.SS2.p1.1.6 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} could exhibit
discrimination against different demographic groups
([e.g.]{#A6.SS2.p1.1.7 .ltx_text .ltx_font_italic}, gender, race,
ethnicity) due to the bias existing in the training datasets. In other
words, [LIFT]{#A6.SS2.p1.1.8 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} may prefer
certain groups while making decisions in downstream tasks, especially
when feature names and different demographic contexts are fed at
training and inference time. This effect is exacerbated by the use of
large pretrained LMs ([i.e.]{#A6.SS2.p1.1.9 .ltx_text .ltx_font_italic},
GPT-J and GPT-3), which have been known to inherently contain bias
\[[178](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib178){.ltx_ref}\].
The bias in the pretraining data for these large language models adds an
opaque layer to regression and classification tasks beyond bias within
the downstream data. Therefore, adopting [LIFT]{#A6.SS2.p1.1.10
.ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} in tasks that consider demographic
information requires more consideration to avoid discrimination. To
further remove the bias, users can combine [LIFT]{#A6.SS2.p1.1.11
.ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} with the existing fairness-aware
reweighting
mechanisms \[[179](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib179){.ltx_ref},
[180](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib180){.ltx_ref}\]
or data augmentation and parameter-efficient fine-tuning
techniques \[[181](https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/2206.06565#bib.bib181){.ltx_ref}\].
Finally, we emphasize that more model evaluation steps are required when
applying [LIFT]{#A6.SS2.p1.1.12 .ltx_text .ltx_font_smallcaps} instead
of using it as a panacea for all applications. We believe our work can
significantly benefit society by providing a simple tool for handling
various tasks with proper justification.

::: {.ltx_pagination .ltx_role_newpage}
